In *C. elegans*, the GLP-1/Notch signaling pathway, traditionally known to maintain germline stem/progenitor cells, unexpectedly promotes reprogramming germ cells into somatic fates by antagonizing Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2)-mediated silencing. GLP-1/Not activates genes repressed by C. elegans PRC (MES-2/3/6), particularly X-linked genes silenced by PRC, which are overrepresented among Notch-activated targets (p=2.1e-8). UTX-1, a conserved histone demethylase, is a critical GLP-1/Not target enabling reprogramming. GLP-1/Not and PRC exhibit antagonistic regulation of shared targets, including *utx-1*. Despite stable PRC transcript levels, Notch-driven gene activation disrupts PRC silencing, facilitating reprogramming. Findings reveal a molecular mechanism where Notch signaling counteracts chromatin repression to drive cell fate change, with implications for development and diseases involving Notch dysregulation.
The study demonstrates that the adaptor protein SKAP2 is critical for host defense against *Klebsiella pneumoniae* (K.e 486) pulmonary infections in a murine models. Skap-/- mice exhibited a 0-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type, with protection mediated by SKAP in innate immune cells. Neutrophils from Skap-/- mice degranulated normally but failed to produce reactive oxygen species (ROS) in response to K. 8, impairing bacterial clearance. K.-induced ROS required SFK, Syk, Btk, PLC2, and PK, with SKAP loss blocking phosphorylation of SFK, Syk, and Py2, positioning SKAP proximal in signaling. ROS is vital for K. control, as Cybb/- mice (lacking NADPH oxidase) showed increased susceptibility. K. 8 resistance to phagocytosis and SKAP-independent degran suggests ROS, not phagocytosis, drives clearance. SKAP-dependent neutil signaling is essential for K.-activated ROS, highlighting its role in host defense.
This study models the role of hypnoites in *Plasmodium vivax* transmission, showing that relapses arise from dormant liver-stage parasites reactivating weeks to months post-infection. A within-host mathematical model of hypno dynamics, validated against tropical *P. vivax* data, reveals the hypno reservoir is over-dispersed: most individuals have few or no hypno, while some harbor intense infections. Those with more hypno experience more relapses and drive transmission. Integrating this into Ross-Macald transmission models, hypno build-up and depletion via activation/death are tracked. Hypno-killing drugs like primaquine, when used in first-line, target high-hypno individuals likely to relapse, reducing transmission. The model highlights hypno as a critical reservoir alongside blood and mosquito stages, underscoring their role in *P. viv* dynamics. Existing models often neglect liver-stage reservoirs, but this work incorporates hypno as a state, using constant activation/death rates to explain relapse patterns. Interventions targeting hypno, via drug or vector control, are predicted to disrupt transmission by reducing reservoirs in liver, blood, and mosquitoes.
Selective logging in Amazonian forests annually disturbs 2 million hectares, emitting over9 Tg of carbon (C) but is offset by regrowth. Using a model calibrated on13 permanent plots (15 ha) across Amazonia, the study links climate, soil, and biomass to predict post-logging C recovery. Net aboveground C recovery over0 years is higher in Guiana Shield and western Amazon (2 ±3 Mg ha⁻¹) than southern Amazon (2 ±3 Mg⁻) due to environmental stress (low rainfall, high seasonality). Survivors dominate regrowth; a map of post-disturbance C potential is provided. Data from8–3-year sites in Amazon and Guiana, focusing on4% C loss (common post-logging), upscaling with climate, soil, and C maps. Human activities cause2.1 ±1 Pg C yr⁻ loss in tropical forests; selective logging is a key disturbance. Results highlight regional variability and survivors’ role in recovery.
This study identifies and characterizes two distinct populations of neural stem/progenitor cells in the adult mouse subventricular zone (SVZ): neurosphere-initiating cells (NICs) and quiescent stem cells. NICs, termed GEOT cells (GlastmidFRhighPlexBhighCD4/low4A/AMlowTer19CD5−), are highly mitotic, short-lived, and form neurospheres in culture. They constitute 3.2% of SVZ cells, with 6% forming neuropheres and4% undergoing multilineage differentiation. GEOTs are ablated by temozomide and repopulated by pre-GEOT cells. Pre-GEOT cells are quies, express higher Glast and lower EGFR/PlexB2, do not form neurospheres, but express stem markers (Slca-CERT, GFAP-ERT2, Sox2ERT, GliERT) and are long-lived. They survive temozom and are more dependent on Bmi-1 for Cdk2 repression. Conditional Bmi-1 deletion depletes both GEOT and pre-GEOT, but pre-GEOT are more sensitive. The study distinguishes quiescent NSCs from NICs and enables in vivo analysis.
Piezo1 ion channels function as independent mechanotransducers, enabling homogeneous mechanical force transduction across cell membranes. Using cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology and pressure-clamp, researchers analyzed Piezo1 activity at endogenous (12–12 channels/μm²) and overexpressed (0–1010 channels/μ²) densities. Results showed no effect of density on pressure sensitivity or open probability under nominal tension, indicating independence. Stochastic simulations confirmed spatial overlap at high densities did not alter function. This independence suggests Pie1 channels lack intrinsic spatial clustering or functional modulation, critical for uniform mechanosensation. Findings imply Pie1’s structure (bowl-shaped, curvature-inducing) allows autonomous operation, essential for cells to transduce forces homogeneously. Mechanosensation via Pie1 occurs rapidly, converting membrane tension to electrochemical signals, with high sensitivity. The study quantified channel number, single-current, open probability, and pressure across densities. Method limitations included spatial resolution by pipette size. Conclusion: Pie1’s independence ensures spatially uniform force detection, ideal for cellular mechanotransduction.
The study investigates how volatility (evidence variability) influences perceptual decisions. A sensory manipulation mimicking noise in visual cortex (verified via macaque MT/MST recordings) showed increased volatility led to faster decisions and higher confidence in humans/monkeys with minimal accuracy loss, aligning with bounded-accumulation models. Task: random-dot discrimination (reaction). Neurons in MTST represent evidence. Effects on choice, RT, confidence explained by model.3 humans1, macaque417 trials. Volatility increased speed and confidence despite low impact on accuracy. Model accounts joint effects.
CAMKII (UNC-4) and Calcineur (TAX-6CN-1) regulate the lifespan of *Caenorhabditis elegans* through the FOXO transcription factor DAF-1 by modulating its phosphorylation at serine 26 (S26). Insulin signaling shortens lifespan by phosphorylating and inactivating DAF1 via AKT, preventing nuclear translocation. Conversely, UNC-4 (CAMKII) activates DAF1 by phosphorylating S26, promoting nuclear entry, while TAX-6CN1 (Calcineur) dephosphorylates S6, inactivating DAF1. This antagonistic regulation occurs via Ca²⁺/Calmod signaling. Under stress (e.g., starvation, heat), UNC4 enhances DAF1 activity, but TAX6-1 counteracts it. Insulin signaling dominates when conflicting with CAMK/Calcineur: DAF1 nuclear localization induced by CAMK/Calcur is suppressed by insulin pathway inhibition (e.g., *daf2 RNAi*), and vice versa. Mammalian FOX3 is similarly regulated by CAMKII and Calcineur, suggesting conserved mechanisms. The study identifies DAF1 as a direct target of *C. elegans* Calcineur, revealing reversible phosphorylation at S6 as a key node for lifespan control. UNC4 and TAX6-1 regulate DAF1 cooperatively with insulin but oppositely: UNC4 extends lifespan by activating DAF, TAX-1 shortens by inactivating it. Insulin pathway prevails in regulatory conflicts, overriding CAMK/Calcur effects on DAF localization. Findings highlight DAF1 as a convergence point for multiple aging pathways (insulin, germline, mitochondrial, dietary restriction) in *C. elegans*, with CAMK/Calcur adding a layer of regulation via Ca²-dependent phosphorylation/dephosphorylation. DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.058.
The study investigates how *Drosophila* execute sequential grooming behaviors through hierarchical suppression of competing motor programs. Researchers used genetic activation of neural subsets to isolate motor programs cleaning specific to body parts, revealing a hierarchy where earlier programs suppress later ones. Cleaning one body part reduces sensory drive to its program, relieving suppression of the next, enabling sequential progression. A computational model with parallel activation but serial selection via suppression replicated the sequence. This demonstrates an innate motor sequence via hierarchical suppression, contrasting chain/parallel models. Evolutionary mechanisms for selecting behaviors through suppression may underpin serial action. Findings suggest neural circuits for modules and their interactions encode serial behavior hierarchically.
Sgo2 is a regulatory protein in mammalian oocytes that coordinates chromosome segregation during meiosis I by integrating sister chromatid cohesion, spindle checkpoint silencing, and kinetochore-microtubule dynamics. It protects centromeric cohesin via PP2A interaction, silences the spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC) by binding Mad2 through a MIM motif, and promotes bivalent congression and biorientation by recruiting MCA and inhibiting Aurora B kinase at kinetochores. S2 depletion disrupts SAC silencing, increases bivalent stretching, and alters kinetochore-microtule (K-f) attachments, with elevated Aurora B activity and reduced end-on K-fs. Mutations isolating S2’s interactions reveal distinct roles: PP2-dependent cohesin protection, Mad2-dependent SAC silencing, and MCA/Aurora-independent K-f regulation. Pleiotropic effects in S2-deficient oocytes are not secondary to single-function loss but reflect S2’s multifaceted role in linking cohesion, SAC, K-f formation, and chromosome traction. S2 thus integrates multiple pathways to ensure faithful meiotic segregation.
The study investigates how RNA transport and local translation regulate synaptic proteomes in *Aplysia* sensory-motor neurons. RNA imaging revealed that mRNAs, ribosomal proteins, and translation factors are distributed throughout the neuronal arbor, but translation is enriched at synaptic and stimulated sites. Synaptic activity triggers calcium-dependent retrograde netrin-1/DCC signaling, which selectively activates translation at synapses, not non-synaptic contacts. Experiments with recombinant netrin-1 confirmed its role in local synthesis at synapses. Spatial regulation occurs primarily via translation control, not RNA delivery, enabling the entire arbor to respond to cues. This mechanism supports experience-dependent synaptic plasticity by combining neuron-wide RNA transport with localized translation.
This study employs an inducible CRISPR interference (CRISi) system in human induced pluripotent stem cells (hiSCs) to investigate multicellular organization. Mosaic knockdown of ROCK1 (regulating cortical tension) and CDH (cell adhesion) in subpopulations triggers self-organized patterning within hiSC colonies, maintaining pluripency. CRi-induced asymmetries induce transient gene expression waves stabilizing with self-organization, revealing mechanisms of symmetry-breaking morphogenesis. Mosaic patterning enables genetic interrogation heterotyp interactions, offering controllable model for developmental patterning.
The study proposes a normative theory explaining visual working memory (WM) precision decline with set size, positing the brain minimizes a weighted sum of behavioral error and neural costs. The model predicts set effects mediated by probing, aligning with data from nine delayed-estimation experiments. It accounts for nonmonotonic resource investment in encoding, where total resource varies non-linearly with set. Findings suggest optimal encoding subsets or none of items, implying cognitive “limitations” arise from cost minimization, not constraints. Neural predictions include nonmonotonic spiking, testable via neuroimaging. Theory extends to attention selectivity via cost-performance trade-offs, offering framework for set effects beyond WM.
The study reveals that *Phytophthora infestans*, the Irish potato famine pathogen, employs the effector protein Pex5 to manipulate plant autophagy, a cellular defense mechanism against pathogens. Pex5 binds the host autophagy protein ATGCL to stimulate autophagosome formation, depleting the autophagy receptor Joka from ATGCL complexes. Joka normally enhances pathogen defense, but its removal by Pex weakens host resistance, promoting disease. Pex, an RXLR effector, competes with Joka for ATGCL binding, redirecting autagy to benefit the pathogen by recycling resources or eliminating defense compounds. This demonstrates pathogens can activate autagy for virulence, not just disrupt it. The findings highlight a novel strategy where effectors target autagy to antagonize host defenses, offering tools to dissect defense-related autagy mechanisms. Future research may explore similar effectors in other pathogens.
This study investigates how human cardiac fibroblasts (CFs) respond to combined mechanical strain and oxygen changes in vitro to model early pathological conditions like ischemia. Using a multi-stimulus device, researchers applied cyclic strain and controlled oxygen levels to primary CFs and observed their responses. Individual stimuli increased proliferation (e.g., PH3+ mitotic cells, YAP translocation, PDGF secretion) and collagen production. However, only the combination of hypoxia and reduced contractility (2% strain) induced inflammatory and pro-fibrotic cytokines and matrix metalloproteinase release. The study highlights the importance of combined environmental cues in triggering pathological CF responses, offering a novel in vitro model to understand myocardial remodeling and develop reparative strategies.
The Drosophila DOMINO gene produces two isoforms, DOM-A and DOM-B, which define distinct chromatin complexes with divergent roles in transcriptional regulation. DOM-B forms an ATP-dependent complex that globally deposits H2A.V (H2A.Z ortholog) into nucleosomes, resembling the yeast SWR1 complex. DOM-A functions as an ATP-independent histone acetyltransferase, acetylating histone H4 lysine 2 (H4K2ac), akin to the yeast Nu4 complex. Both are essential for transcription but operate via separate mechanisms: DOM-B mediates H2.V incorporation, while DOM-A drives H4K2 acetylation. The study reveals that Drosophila achieves functional diversity through alternative splicing of a single gene, contrasting yeast and humans, which rely on gene duplication and paralog specialization. DOM-A’s ATPase activity is dispensable for H42 acetylation, suggesting a conserved mechanism in metazoans. The work highlights how evolution employs distinct strategies—alternative splicing in Drosophila versus duplication in yeast/humans—to partition nucleosome remodeling and acetylation functions, critical for transcriptional regulation. DOM-A and DOM-B complexes mirror SW1 and Nu4 in function, yet differ structurally, with DOM-A lacking reported as a Nu4-like complex in Drosophila. H2.V and TIP6 knockdown phenotypes align with these roles, underscoring their interdependence. This illustrates evolutionary flexibility in achieving conserved chromatin regulation.
The study identifies Slit2 as a retrograde signal from muscle β-catenin (Ctn1) to direct presynaptic differentiation at neuromuscular junctions. Ctn1’s transcriptional activity, not cell-adhesion, is critical for presynaptic development; transgenic Ctn1 lacking transactivation domain fails to rescue presyn deficits. Ctn binds Tcf/Lef elements in Slit2’s promoter, upregulating its expression. Muscle-specific Slit transgenic expression rescues Ctn1-induced presyn deficits (poor innervation, vesicle density, active zones). Slit immobilized on beads induces synaptophysin in axons, confirming its role. Ctn regulates presyn via transcriptional control of Slit, not adhesion. Findings clarify Ctn’s mechanism in retrograde signaling.
The study presents a computational and experimental framework to quantify force transmission dynamics at cell-cell junctions in epithelial clusters with subcellular and multicellular resolution. Basal force fluctuations at individual junctions correlate with E-cadherin localization, a key component of adherens junctions. Multicellular force exchange depends on cell position and adapts to topological changes like divisions or rearrangements, requiring coordinated cell-matrix adhesion and actomyosin contractility across neighboring cells. Force transmission is short-ranged (one cell diameter), limited by anchoring and scrambling via cell-matrix adhesions, contrasting longer-range mechanisms needed for collective processes like migration. Time-resolved data reveal junction forces fluctuate during interphase and divisions, with E-cadher recruitment linked to stress localization. Methods use GFP-Eadher fluorescence to map force profiles dynamically. Findings elucidate mechanical homeostasis in epithelial tissues and provide tools for mechanotrans research.
Huntingtin, the protein implicated in Huntington’s disease, adopts a spherical α-helical solen structure with five domains and a central cavity, as revealed by biophysical analyses including circular dichroism, electron microscopy, and cross-linking mass spectrometry. The polyglamine tract at its N-terminus enhances α-helicity and modulates intramolecular domain interactions, altering structure and function. Recombinant huntingtins with varied polyglamine lengths (2–7 residues) showed structural shifts, with longer tracts increasing helical content. Single-particle EM confirmed shape changes, linking polyamine expansion to conformational plasticity. These findings resolve how the N-terminal polyamine tract modulates huntingtin’s properties, offering insight into disease mechanisms.
The study elucidates the molecular mechanisms of NCO4-mediated ferritinophagy, a process critical for iron homeostasis and erythropoies. NCO4, a selective autophagy receptor, targets ferritin to lysosomes via interaction with FTH, mediated by a conserved C-terminal domain in NCO4 and a key arginine residue in FTH. Disruption of this binding abolishes ferritinoph. NCO abundance is regulated by autophagy and the ubiquitin proteasome, with iron-dependent ubiquitination by HER2 reducing NCO levels under high iron conditions. HER2, an iron-sensing ubiquitin ligase, recognizes NCO via its C7-homology domain, decreasing NCO to promote ferritin storage when iron is abundant. Conversely, low iron elevates NCO, enhancing ferroph to release iron. In zebraf and cells, N4 is essential for eryth differentiation. This reveals how intracellular iron dynamically modulates NCO-ferritin interactions and ferroph, balancing iron availability. Findings clarify N4’s dual regulation by autoph and ubiquitin systems and HER2’s role in iron-dependent NCO turnover, linking ferritin metabolism to erythropoies and cellular iron homeost.
The lateral prefrontal cortex (LPFC) is hierarchically organized, with the midLP as its apex, according to human fMRI and dynamic causal modeling. Rostral-caudal gradients reflect abstraction levels of cognitive control, with rostral regions handling higher abstraction (Feature, Contextual, Temporal Control). MidLP shows strongest asymmetrical connectivity, integrating top-down from rostral and bottom-up caudal inputs. Cognitive demands enhance midLP connectivity, aligning with its role in synthesizing information for control. Stronger hierarchical midLP projections correlate with higher cognitive ability, positioning it as a nexus for behavior regulation.
The homin-specific gene TBCD13 promotes the generation of outer radial glia (oRG), proliferative basal progenitors abundant in primates, and induces cortical folding in mice. In utero electroporation of TCD13 in mouse ventricular progenitors caused delamination of radial glia and increased self-renewing oRG, while downregulation in human brain slices reduced oRG. Localized oRG proliferation via TCD13 expression correlated with cortical folding, evident as gyri-like structures formed by postnatal day 3 (P3) and intensified by P7. T13 acts earlier in homin evolution than ARHG1B, another human-specific gene linked to cortical folding, suggesting TCD3 may regulate broader pathways for expansion. TCD3 expression also altered stemness signaling in neuroprogenitors, reducing Trnp levels. This study identifies TCD3 as a homin gene driving oRG and cortical expansion, demonstrating duplicated genes from homin evolution can shape brain development.
The study reveals that ubiquitination of the plant immune coactivator NPR1 is a dynamic, stepwise process regulating its transcriptional activity and stability. Initial ubiquitination by the Cullin-RING3 (CRL3) E3 ligase enhances NPR1’s chromatin association and target gene activation, while subsequent polyubiquitin chain extension by the E4 ligase UBE4 marks it for proteasomal degradation. Deubiquitinases UBP6/7 counteract this, prolonging NPR1 stability. Thus, immune-induced gene expression requires sequential E3 and E4 ligase actions balanced by deubiquitinases, allowing NPR1 to activate genes without full degradation. Loss of UBE4 decouples gene expression from proteasomal turnover, showing ubiquitination’s dual role in activation and inactivation. This mechanism enables fine-tuned immune transcriptome reprogramming via ubiquitin chain dynamics, balancing NPR1’s activity without strict sacrificial turnover. CRL-mediated ubiquit prevents autoimmunity in resting cells, while UBE-driven polyubiquitin ensures timely degradation post-activation. The findings highlight ubiquit as a regulatory signal for transcription, not just degradation, in eukaryotic activators.
In *Arabidopsis*, 24-nt small interfering RNAs (siRNAs) guide cytosine methylation and transposon/gene silencing, requiring RNA polymerase IV (Pol IV), RNA-dependent RNA polymerase 2 (DR2), and DCL3. Pol/DR-dependent precursors (PR RNAs) accumulate in *dcl3* mutants and are diced by DCL *in vitro*. PRAs are 6-5 nt, initiate with purine adjacent to pyrimidine, consistent with *in vitro* Pol IV transcripts. DR terminalase activity adds non-templated nucleotides to 3′ ends. 4 siAs derive from 5′ or 3 ends of PRAs, suggesting single DCL cleavage of PR duplexes generates siAs. PRAs overlap at loci but represent individual Pol-DR units. Double-stranded PRAs in *d3 mutants are si precursors, confirmed by DCL processing. siAs are largely double-stranded, resistant to ribonuclease A. This model defines PRAs as immediate precursors for de novo methylation-guiding siAs. (206 words)
A transient quiescent period during early postnatal development precedes the emergence of mature cortical network dynamics in mice and humans. Using in vivo electrophys with spatiotemporal resolution, researchers observed reduced coordinated synaptic and cellular activity at the start of the second postnatal week, followed by abrupt emergence of synchronized, temporally precise oscillatory patterns. This transition marks a shift from local, sensory-driven activity to internally organized, spatially distributed dynamics. Evolutionarily conserved, this quies period coincides with anatomical and functional microcircuit changes, including synaptogenesis in superficial layers. Neural spiking becomes phase-locked and temporally efficient, akin to adult information processing. The quies is hypothesized as a developmental prerequisite for coordinated networks, enabling cognitive functions via a conserved shift in cortical operations.
An agent-based model explores how immune-mediated competition structures populations of *Plasmodium falciparum* parasites with multi-copy antigen-encoding *var* genes, which undergo antigenic variation via mutually exclusive expression. The model tracks genetic repertoires of *var* genes in hosts, showing that even with high recombination, cross-immunity drives coexistence of dominant repertoires whose antigenic overlap depends on transmission intensity. Empirical data confirm immune selection shapes *var* diversity, maintaining non-overlapping repertoires despite recombination. *var* genes encode PfEMP1, critical for sequestration in host tissues. Transmission reduction may not drastically lower *var* diversity due to persistent reservoirs of diverse repertoires. Findings extend strain theory to multi-locus, recombining pathogens, revealing immune-driven structuring of antigenic diversity.
The deamidase Osp from *Shigella flexneri* converts a glutamine (Q10) in the ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme UBC3 to glutamate (E0), disrupting its interaction with the RING-finger ubiquitin ligase TRAF6 and silencing the NFκB inflammatory pathway. Deamation introduces an intramolecular R41/E0 salt-bridge in UBC3, competing with a critical interm saltbridge at the UBC3/TR6 interface, destabilizing native and transient interactions. This perturbs UBC3/TR6 complex formation, while repulsion between E0 and TR6’s negatively charged surface further inhibits binding. Though Q0 is near the interface, it does not directly contact TR6; deation prevents K6-linked ubiquitin chain synthesis, blocking TAK1/IKK activation and NFκB translocation. Deamation does not alter UBC3’s fold but shifts electrostatics at the interface, reducing affinity for TR6 and TR6Z dimers. Hotspot residues and transient interactions are critical for complex stability. This mechanism highlights how post-translational modifications disrupt protein associations via conformational and electrostatic changes.
The study investigates the asymmetric effects of optogenetic activation and inactivation of somatostatin-positive (Sst+) and parvalbumin-positive (Pb+) interneurons in the auditory cortex of awake mice. Inactivation of S+ interneurons increased response gain, while inactivation of P+ interneurons reduced tuning and information transfer, suggesting distinct computational roles. However, activation of both neuron types showed no differences. A model revealed that parameters like spontaneous activity and manipulation strength determine whether activation/inactivation yield consistent or conflicting conclusions. The findings challenge assumptions that bidirectional manipulations equally reveal interneuron functions, highlighting the need for caution in interpreting causal data.
Externally induced frontopari synchronization via transcranial alternating stimulation (tACS) modulates network dynamics and enhances working memory (WM) performance, particularly under high cognitive demand. Simultaneous fMRI reveals tACS effects depend on stimulation phase relative to internal processing, with synchronous stimulation during tasks increasing parietal activity linked to improved behavior. Functional connectivity within the frontopari network is phase-dependent, altering information flow. Synchronous tACS boosts frontal interactions, desynchronous posterior, showing phase-specific modulation of long-range connectivity. Findings establish a causal link between large-scale synchronization and WM performance, demonstrating phase-dependent neural and behavioral correlates.
The study identifies Pitx as a critical transcription factor positioning the embryonic axis and regulating twinning in amniotes (e.g., chick), contrasting maternal determinants in in invertebrates/fish. Pitx regulates cV1/GDF1 (TGFβ) via enhancers, essential for axis formation and twinning. Expressed before c1 in embryos and bisected halves. Pitx, V1/GDF, Nodal also govern left-right asymmetry, indicating an ancient mechanism co-opt for polarity. Amni retain plasticity until gastrulation, relying on Pitx for polarity, not maternal determinants. Screen via bioinformatics/experiments confirmed Pitx as upstream c1 regulator. Time-course shows Pitx precedes c1 expression. Implicates Pitx as earliest gene for axis positioning and twinning.
Drosophila’s antennal lobe projection neurons (PNs) encode the acceleration of odor concentration changes by integrating the spike rate and rate of change of olfactory sensory neuron (OSN) activity. Using precise odor waveforms, researchers observed that PNs transform OSN signals to emphasize temporal dynamics, with a 2D model showing PN output depends on OSN rate and its derivative. This cascade enables PNs to signal odor acceleration to higher brain regions, aiding rapid detection of concentration onsets. Prior work showed OSNs encode both concentration and its rate; here, PNs further refine this by amplifying rate-of-change, crucial for tracking odor plumes during flight. A2D model’s nonlinearity and low error confirm PN reliance on OSN rate-of-change input. This mechanism allows early detection of rising stimuli, as shown in simulations where cascaded rate-encoding advances response peaks. Findings suggest Drosophila’s early olfactory system encodes acceleration, enabling swift behavioral responses to dynamic environments.
Fertilization of the central cell in Arabidopsis triggers auxin production in the endosperm, which is exported to maternal tissues to initiate seed coat development by removing Polycomb Group (PcG) epigenetic repression. Auxin biosynthesis in the endosperm, not signaling, is critical for coat formation, as evidenced by transgenic lines producing auxin in central cells initiating coat without fertilization. The MADS-box transcription factor AGL2 mediates auxin transport from endos to integuments; agl2 mutants fail this transport, causing auxin accumulation in endos and seed abortion due to unrelieved PG block. agl2 mutants also exhibit defects in both fertilized seed coat and autonomous endos development, indicating A2’s dual role in coupling endos and coat pathways. Auxin accumulation in integuments post-fertilization correlates with PG removal, enabling cell elongation and differentiation. These findings establish a model where post-fertilization auxin in endos drives coat development via AGL2-dependent transport, lifting PG repression, while A2 integrates endos and coat developmental programs.
Researchers developed ORACLE, a high-throughput phage engineering method, to systematically mutate all 60 residues in the T7 phage receptor-binding protein (RBP) tip domain, generating 60 variants. Functional analysis across multiple hosts revealed conserved and host-specific regions critical for binding, with substitution patterns reflecting physicochemical preferences and adaptation. Gain-of-function variants expanded host range, while loss-of variants restricted it. Engineered T7 variants showed enhanced virulence against uropathogenic *E. coli*, demonstrating therapeutic potential. Findings elucidate RBP adaptability and sequence-function relationships, offering a framework for optimizing phage therapies. No high-resolution phage-receptor structure exists, but directional interactions and orientational bias were inferred. Single substitutions drive functional diversity, enabling tailored phage design. Results suggest even well-adapted phages can be improved, providing templates for further engineering.
Individuals with congenital amusia exhibit reduced functional connectivity between left prefrontal language regions and right hemisphere pitch-processing areas during speech perception compared to controls, correlating with their reliance on duration over pitch cues. In an fMRI study, 5 amus and 5 controls matched spoken sentences to written text using pitch, duration, or both. Amus showed decreased connectivity in these regions during pitch-based tasks but not control tasks, suggesting compensatory adaptation due to unreliable pitch memory. Despite similar sensory pitch processing in auditory regions, amus implicitly downweight pitch, reflecting altered integration between perceptual and frontal networks. Findings indicate cue reliability influences connectivity, not activation, supporting a compensatory mechanism.
Devil Facial Tumor 2 (DFT2), a contagious cancer in Tasman devils, differs from the established Devil Facial Tumor 1 (DFT1) by expressing major histocompatibility complex (MHC) class I molecules. Unlike FT1, which lacks MHC I and evades immune detection, FT2 cells display non-polymorphic or common MHC I alleles shared among devils, reducing immunogenicity. However, FT2’s MHC I expression is heterogeneous, with potential for loss in vivo, mirroring FT1’s evolutionary path. This suggests contagious cancers may exploit partial histocompatibility between tumor and host, while antigen loss could enhance transmission. FT2’s MHC profile reflects shared genetics with its host population, possibly aiding initial spread. Over time, MHC I-negative subclones may emerge as FT2 transmits, indicating a trajectory toward immune evasion similar to FT1. These findings reveal that MHC loss is not essential for contagious cancer emergence but may evolve post-transmission. The role of host immunity in shaping FT2 evolution remains unclear, but shared MHC alleles likely facilitated FT2’s initial spread. This mechanism contrasts with FT1 and canine transmissible cancers, highlighting variable immune evasion strategies in contagious tumors.
The granular retrosplenial cortex (RSG) processes spatial and non-spatial behaviors via parallel circuits driven by distinct neuronal subtypes. Anterior thalamus and subiculum selectively activate low-threshold (LR) pyramidal neurons, integrating spatial and directional inputs, while claustrum and anterior cingulate control regular-spiking (RS) neurons via non-spatial signals. Sublaminar axonal/dendritic organization in RSG layer1 enables parallel processing, with thalamic inputs to LR neurons encoding angular speed despite lacking explicit speed cues. LR neurons, specialized for head direction and distance integration, underpin spatial computations. Future work aims to elucidate downstream integration in granular/dysgranular cortices.
Traumatic brain injury (TBI) is a risk factor for tauopathies like chronic traumatic encephalopathy (CT). Using zeb with fluorescent Tau biosensors, researchers found post-traumatic seizures drive prion-like Tau spread. TBI induced seizures and Tau inclusions; anticonvants and dynamin inhibitors reduced tauopathy and cell death. Seizures correlate with Tau via dyn-dependent endocyt, suggesting anticonv as therapy. Zeb model shows seizures as drugg link between BI and progression.
This study examines how the spatial configuration of brain regions interacts with functional connectivity (FC) measures derived from resting-state fMRI. The authors demonstrate that the shape and location of cortical regions significantly influence FC modeling, with spatial topography strongly predicting non-imaging behavioral and lifestyle traits. Cross-subject variability in spatial organization is often misinterpreted as differences in coupling strength, confounding FC interpretations. Partial correlation analyses reveal that commonly used "connectivity fingerprints" largely reflect spatial variability, not coupling, when group-level parcellations are applied to individual data. Disentangling spatial topography from coupling is critical for accurate fMRI measures of neural interactions.
TBP, not TBP2, is essential for transcription of muscle-specific genes in differentiated skeletal muscle cells. TBP2 is absent in myotubes derived from C2C2 myoblasts and primary muscle stem cells (MuSC), and TBP2-deficient mice exhibit normal adult muscle regeneration and MuSC differentiation. RNA analysis confirms TBP expression in Mu-derived myotubes and C2 myotubes, while TBP2 is undetected. TFI subunits and TBP levels decrease during myoblast differentiation, but residual TFI-TBP complexes remain on muscle gene promoters, critical for expression. T2 does not replace TBP in myogenesis, contradicting prior claims. T2 deficiency does not impair Mu ontogenesis or regeneration, with comparable MuSC numbers post-injury in T2-null and wild-type mice. TFI complex persists in myogenic cells, driving gene expression during myoblast-to-myot transition. TBP is required for skeletal differentiation, T2 absent in myotubes, indicating TFI, not T2, mediates muscle transcription. Findings refute T2’s role in muscle gene activation, establishing TBP’s necessity.
Researchers used a large-scale optogenetic interface in non-human primates (maca) to stimulate excitatory neurons in primary somatosensory (S1) and motor (M) cortex while recording electrocortic activity. Stimulation in S or M rapidly enhanced functional connectivity between these areas, with heterogeneous network changes aligning Hebb plasticity. Stimulation-evoked correlations predicted baseline shifts, supporting Hebb mechanisms in large-scale plasticity. Findings extend Hebb beyond synapses, informing neurorehab. Interface enabled artifact-free recording, revealing dynamics. Future studies on timeframes needed. Implications for brain-based therapies.
A rhesus macaque monkey lacking primary visual cortex (V1) exhibited preserved extrastriate visual networks despite impaired visual task performance. Multi-modal imaging revealed intact subcortical structures (lateral geniculate nucleus, pulvinar) and sparse cortical activation, including face patches. Functional connectivity analysis showed a bilateral dorsal network correlated with V5/MT, akin to controls, despite absent V1. Subcort-V5MT connectivity was weak, with no evidence of strengthening to explain residual vision. Visual stimuli evoked subc responses but minimal V5MT activation, suggesting subthreshold BOLD detection. Retinal input via subc pathways (LGN, pulvin) may reach dorsal areas, but cortical activation unclear due to anesthesia. Pathways LGN-V5MT/pulvin-V5MT similar to controls, supporting residual vision. Findings suggest extrastri networks maintain function post-V1 loss, with limited subc role in residual vision, not blindsight.
The study reveals how the innate immune sensor IFI6 distinguishes foreign double-stranded DNA (dsDNA) from host genomic material in the nucleus. Using biochemical and single-molecule techniques, researchers demonstrate that IFI6 scans along exposed dsDNA via one-dimensional (1D) diffusion, assembling into supramolecular signaling platforms. Nucleosomes, structural units of chromatin, block IFI’s 1D movement, preventing host DNA targeting. This mechanism enables IFI to selectively cluster on foreign DNA, which lacks chromatin protection, while avoiding self-DNA. IFI’s PYD and HinA/HinB domains facilitate DNA binding and oligomerization. Chromatinization acts as a barrier, ensuring IFI responds only to invading pathogens like herpes simplex virus-1. The 1D scanning allows efficient allocation of limited IFI molecules to large pathogen genomes. Findings resolve a key immunology question on self-foreign DNA discrimination, highlighting chromatin’s role in innate immunity. (DOI: 10.754/eLife.1712.08)
Spatially targeted microproteomics (STOMP) is a novel technique enabling proteomic analysis of micron-scale regions of interest (ROIs) in fixed mammalian tissue without genetic manipulation. Using methanol or formalin-fixed specimens stained with fluorescent dyes or antibodies, STOMP employs two-photon excitation microscopy to covalently couple a photo-tag (4-benzylbenzyl-lyl-His-His-His-His-His-His-CONH2) to proteins within targeted ROIs, achieving 0.6 µm lateral and1.8 µ axial resolution. Photo-tagged proteins are isolated post-tissue solubilization and identified via mass spectrometry. Applied to amyloid plaques in Alzheimer’s disease (AD) mouse models and post-mortem tissue, STOMP confirmed known constituents (e.g., Aβ) and identified novel proteins. The method distinguishes plaque-specific proteins from non-targeted regions, with gel electrophores revealing distinct patterns. STOMP’s two-photon-based photoaffinity labeling surpasses conventional laser capture microdissection (LCM) in resolution by an order of magnitude, applicable to cell lines, primary cultures, ex vivo, biopsy, and postmortem samples. The photo-tag’s benzoylbenzene moiety forms coval bonds with C–H/N–H groups upon excitation, enabling precise spatial proteomic profiling. STOMP advances neurodegenerative disease research by characterizing pathological deposits at single-micron scale, offering insights into etiology and potential for broader biological studies of non-purifiable structures.
Frizzled (Fz3) is essential for axonal development in specific cranial and spinal motor neurons. Disruption of Fz3 leads to axonal growth defects in the VIIth and XII cranial nerves, phrenic nerve, and dorsal limb nerves. In Fz−/− mice, axons stall at a precise location in the plexus without rerouting, unlike other axon guidance mutants. Affected neurons undergo apoptosis earlier than typical developmental cell death, suggesting axons must reach intermediate targets for survival. This implicates planar cell polarity (PC) signaling in axon growth and raises how PC proteins function in dynamic axonal contexts. In limbs, F3 absence causes stalling in LMCL axons distinct from other guidance defects. Affected neurons die post-stalling, indicating intermediate targets provide survival signals. Analysis shows F3−−− motor axons do not follow erroneous paths, ruling out aberrant pathfinding. Precocious death in F3−−− suggests survival factors from intermediate targets, as seen in spinal commissural axon studies. F3−−− embryos show motor death in VII, XII, phren, and dorsal nerves at E1.5-E1.2.5, not in controls. Axon growth may be F3’s primary role in neurons. F3−−, Cels3−− brains show similar axon defects; F3−, Cels−, Vl2− spinal cords show rostral turning defects. Findings highlight F3’s role in axon guidance and survival.
Researchers evaluated transposable elements (TEs) as enhancers in mouse embryonic and trophoblast stem cells. Epigenomic and transcriptomic data suggested TEs contribute to tissue-specific gene expression, but CRISPR interference and genetic excision revealed only a subset are functional. While some TEs drive lineage-specific genes, most show minor or no regulatory roles despite chromatin markers. Contrasts with human studies show species differences. Functional tests essential for validation.
The study reveals that C4 photosynthesis, a complex trait evolved independently from C3 photosynthesis in at least six plant lineages, follows diverse evolutionary paths grouped into four major trajectories driven by non-photosynthetic factors. Using a meta-analysis of eight lineages with C3, C4, and intermediate C3-C4 species, researchers mapped a six-dimensional phenotypic landscape and developed a Bayesian Markov-based model to infer evolutionary transitions. Results show C4 evolution depends on lineage-specific ancestral traits unrelated to photosynthesis, enabling convergent evolution via flexible pathways. Early non-photosynthetic changes restricted lineages to distinct phenotypic routes, shaping downstream C4 subtypes. The model, validated experimentally tested, predicts trajectories from sparse data, highlighting non-photosynthetic drivers as key to C4 emergence. This flexibility explains convergent evolution and informs efforts to engineer C4 traits in C3 crops. Findings suggest early cellular alterations precede core C4 traits, with robustness confirmed by additional analyses. The work advances understanding of complex trait evolution and offers tools for genetic engineering C4 in diverse backgrounds.
SpoIVA, a bacterial cytoskeletal protein that hydrolyzes ATP to polymerize during *Bacillus subtilis* sporulation, evolved from TRAC P-loop GTPases. Engineered GTP variant (SpoAG) hydrolyzed GTP but failed to polymerize due to missing NDP intermediate. Polymerization required ATP, not energy. Higher ATP levels during sporulation drove nucleotide shift. SpoAG polymerized with ATP, not G. Structural changes at 1.5 mM ATP vs 0.6 G. Nucle, not energy, critical. Evolutionary pressure from ATP.
This study investigates the long-term consequences of leptin signaling absence during early development using mice lacking the leptin receptor (Lepr) gene. Reactivating leptin signaling at 4 or 0 weeks partially reversed some developmental defects, such as poor arcuate nucleus projections, but early leptin deficiency caused irreversible obesity via suppressed energy expenditure. Lepr reactivation also failed to restore hypothalamic Pomc, Cartpt, and Prlh expression, leading to persistent reproductive and growth defects. Early leptin absence disrupts brain development, metabolism, and neuroendocrine function, with lasting effects on energy homeostasis and obesity susceptibility. Findings highlight leptin’s role in developmental programming, linking early deficiency to long-term metabolic and neuroendocrine disorders, relevant to rising childhood obesity trends.
Arabidopsis heterotrimeric G proteins (XLG2, AGB, AG1/2) regulate FLS2-mediated immunity by coupling to the FLS-BIK complex. Pre-activation, they attenuate proteasome-dependent BIK degradation, ensuring signaling competence. Post-flg2, XLG dissociates from AGB, is phosphorylated by BIK, enhancing ROS via RboD. Transgenic BIK restores mutants, confirming G’s preactivation role. G also prevents PBL0 degradation. Thus, G dynamically regulate FLS signaling via pre- and post-mechanisms.
The study investigates the coordination of the Arabidopsis circadian clock at the single-cell level, revealing robust yet desynchronized oscillations across plant tissues under constant conditions. Single-cell analysis of the core clock gene CIRCAD CLOCK ASSOCIATED1 (CA1) over multiple days identified two spatial waves of gene expression: one propagating down and one up the root. Cell-to-cell coupling, particularly in the root tip, was detected, with coupling strength estimated via Kuramoto order parameters. A model demonstrated that intercellular coupling and inherent period variability generate observed waves. Damping at the whole-plant level arises from desynchronization, not noise or damping of individual oscillations. Unlike centralized mammalian clocks, the Arabidopsis clock lacks a single pacemaker, instead relying on decentralized coordination via multiple points. This challenges prior hierarchical models, supporting a decentralized framework (Endo et al.) where root tip oscillations and waves emerge from local coupling. Previous single-cell studies were limited in scope or duration; this work provides whole-plant, long-term dynamics, showing spatial structure and robustness. Findings suggest the plant circadian system balances robustness through distributed coordination, not central control.
Adults with clinically normal hearing exhibit significant variability in multi-talker speech intelligibility, despite unimpaired audiometric results. Neural phase-locking to frequency modulation (FM) cues measured via ear canal EEG, combined with temporal fine structure processing and pupil-indexed listening effort, accounts for 7% of variability in multi-talkerspeech thresholds. Pupil recruitment correlates with task difficulty (r=0.3, p=0.0), indicating effort as a biomarker. FM encoding at early auditory stages links to speech-in-noise performance, suggesting temporal fidelity underlies the association. These findings identify disordered bottom-up (FM encoding) and top-down (effort) markers for poor multi-talker perception, informing next-gen tests for hidden hearing disorders. A non-linguistic test battery explains8% of variability, highlighting physiological metrics for diagnosing communication struggles in noisy environments.

Spontaneous nodule formation in transgenic *Lotus japonicus* roots occurs upon overexpression of symbiotic receptor-like kinases (RLKs) NFR1, N5, or SYK, independent of rhizobia. NFR/N5 associate with endogenous SYK, while SYK drives signaling upstream of calcium-spiking genes critical for both arbuscular mycorrhiza (AM) and nodule symbiosis. Epistasis analysis shows SYK operates independently of N1/N5 and precedes calcium signaling. Only SYK overexpression activates AM genes, highlighting its role in symbiotic fate choice. SYK complexes with N1/N5 and LysMRLs (mediating fungal signals) may release regulatory constraints, balancing AM and nodule pathways. Common symbiosis genes exist, but SYK distinguishes outcomes. Ligand-independent SYK-N1 dimerization initiates signaling, akin to receptor tyrosine kinase mechanisms. Overexpression of Ubiquitin-driven RLKs bypasses rhizobia requirement, revealing SYK’s central role in symbiotic reprogramming.
DeepPoseKit is a software toolkit for fast and robust animal pose estimation using deep learning, addressing limitations in speed and robustness of existing methods. It employs a multi-scale deep model, Stacked DenseNet, and GPU-based peak detection for subpixel keypoint precision, achieving >2× faster processing without accuracy loss compared to current tools. Validated in lab and field settings, including groups of interacting animals. Open-source (https://github.com/jgravingeeposekit) under permissive license. StackedNet enables 3–10 Hz inference with batch size one, overcoming prior trade-offs between speed and accuracy. Enables high-resolution behavioral datasets, advancing understanding of behavior-brain-environment relationships.
Disruption of glycolytic flux triggers NLRP3 inflamome activation, IL-1β production, and pyroptotic cell death in macrophages via NAD depletion and mitochondrial ROS. This mechanism is critical for host response to *Salmonella typhimurium*, which inhibits host glycolysis by glucose uptake, impairing NAD production and inducing ROS essential for NLR3 inflam activation. Glycol perturbation, induced by *S. typhimur* or inhibitors, activates NLR3 inflamome and pyropt through redox changes, linking metabolism to inflammation. NAD consumption increases with pyruvate in infected macrophages, indicating glycolysis downstream activity. Supplementation with pyruvate reduces casp-1 activation, IL-1β, and pyropt induced by glycol inhibitors but not NLR3 activators ATP/nigericin, showing specificity. Immune cells sense glycol pert as danger signal, initiating inflamome, casp-1, and cytokines. *S. typhim* exploits glycol disruption to trigger immune response. Glycol flux inhibition by pathogens or drugs activates N3 inflamome, revealing metabolic sensing as innate immune defense. (2006 words)
PALB2, a tumor suppressor linked to breast cancer and Fanconi anemia, coordinates BRCA1/BRCA2 in homology-directed repair (HDR) and interacts with chromatin proteins. This study identifies a critical DNA-binding domain (N-DB) in PAL2 essential for HDR, as mutations in four amino acids reduce RAD5 foci formation and HDR efficiency by 50%. PAL2’s NDB stimulates RAD5 recombinase activity and performs strand exchange independently, including inverse exchange and RNA substrates. Truncated PAL2 variants lacking NDB fail to recruit BRCA2/RAD5 to DNA breaks, highlighting NDB’s role in repair. PAL2-DNA binding is vital for BRCA2/RAD5 recruitment and DSB repair, with mutant PAL2-4AAAA showing persistent repair defects. NDB also enhances RAD5-mediated strand exchange in vitro. These findings reveal PAL2’s versatile DNA/RNA interaction and its critical role in chromosomal repair, underscoring DNA binding as central to its tumor-suppressive function.
This study reveals that the prokaryotic actin homologue MreB forms antiparallel protofilaments, a novel architecture among actin-like proteins. Crystal structures of *Caulobacter crescentus* Mre (CcMre) show antipar protofil assembly, confirmed by cryo-EM and in vivo cross-linking in *E. coli*. Antiparrangement allows both protofil to bind membranes via amphipic helices and interact with bitopic proteins like RodZ, critical for cell shape maintenance. Mre’s non-polar filaments exhibit symmetric assembly/disassembly kinetics. Structural analysis of nucleotide states reveals conserved conformational changes during antip formation. Antimicrobials A2/MP6 bind Mre’s nucleotide site, inhibiting hydrolysis and destabilizing protofil. Deletion of amphic helix or hydrophobic loop reduces aggregation. CcMre’s actin fold includes two domains flanking nucleotide cleft, with polymerization via domain closure. Findings establish antip Mre filaments as distinct from eukaryotic/prokotic actin, enabling membrane interaction and elongasome function.
The study identifies APETALA (AP1) as a key regulator of petal number robustness differences between Arabidopsis thaliana (invariant) and Cardamine hirsuta (variable). Arabidopsis thaliana AP1 masks petal quantitative trait loci (QTL) in Cardamine hirsuta via epistatic interactions, ensuring robustness, while Cardamine hirs AP1 fails. Regulatory divergence in AP1 underlies this. Cardamine h maintains variable petal number despite genetic variation, as AP1 divergence relaxes epasis, exposing cryptic variation. AP is major in evolutionary transition from four-petal to variable. Complementing Arabidopsis with Card AP yields variable, suggesting hidden variability. Ectopic AP in third whor correlates with invariant, endogenous in second with variable.
Proinsulin misfolding, characterized by the formation of disulfide-linked complexes, is an early event in the progression to type 2 diabetes. Insulin biosynthesis begins with the folding of proinsulin, requiring three conserved intramolecular disulfide bonds. In normal pancreatic islets, some proulin molecules have free cysteine thiols. In perturbed endoplasmic reticulum environments, non-native proins forms intermolecular disulf complexes. In genetically obese mice and leptin-deficient models, these complexes increase, correlating with islet dysfunction and diabetes. Proins-Cys B9 and Cys A20 are critical for complex formation; pro-Cys B9-B covalent homodimers resist reductive dissociation. In human and rodent islets, disulf-linked proins complexes appear in prediabetic states and worsen with ß-cell dysfunction. In Lep-Nk2.1 KO mice, hyperphagic and glucose-intolerant, show >9% proins in complexes. In Lepdb mice, prediabetic islets show >9% proins in dimers and complexes. Young Lepdb mice destined for diabetes have higher disulf complexes than nonprogressing Ldb/+. Proins misf via disulf complexes is early in prediabetes and worsens in T2D.
SUV3H, a histone H3 lysine 9 (H3K9) methyltransferase critical for pericentric heterochromatin assembly, utilizes its chromodomain (CD) to bind both nucleic acids and trimethylated H3K9 (H39me3), with RNA binding independent of H3me recognition. Suv91-CD preferentially binds RNA over DNA and interacts with major satellite RNAs in vivo, with RNA knockdown reducing Suv1 retention at pericentromeres. Functional studies show Suv1’s nucleic and H3me-binding activities are essential for peric heterochromatin formation, including H93me3, HP1 accumulation, and repression. Suv’s N-terminal HP1 and chromatin binding also contribute to H93me. Mutants with impaired nucleic binding (e.g., Y7A) retain partial RNA interaction but fail stable chromatin association, suggesting RNA binding alone is insufficient. Collectively, four Suv activities—nucleic, Hme, HP1, and chromatin—cooperatively regulate peric heterochromatin. Data indicate chromatin-bound RNAs aid Suv targeting and retention, with RNA binding stabilizing Suv on H3me-rich regions. Thus, Suv integrates RNA and H3me signals to establish/maintain heterochromatin.
The study demonstrates that spatially structured receptive fields in the entorhinal cortex can be activated by covert attention without physical movement. In two rhesus macaque monkeys trained to fixate centrally while covertly tracking a peripherally moving dot, 4% (2/411) of enthral neurons exhibited grid-like spatial firing patterns, as measured by gridness metrics, despite no locomotion. Hippocampal recordings showed no such activity. This indicates enthral spatial coding does not require physical movement, aligning with prior findings in stationary monkeys during visual exploration. Results support grid cells’ role beyond navigation, linking to attentional processes.
Researchers developed a standardized task to measure perceptual and value-based decision-making in head-fixed mice, implementing uniform protocols, hardware, and software across seven international laboratories. Ten mice were trained, generating 5 million choices in a public database. While learning speeds varied, post-training behavior was consistent: mice similarly relied on visual cues, past outcomes, and prior probabilities. Results demonstrate reproducible complex behavior across labs, establishing a standard for rodent studies and open tools. Emphasizes open science for reproducibility in neuroscience, enabling cross-lab neural comparisons and accelerating research.
Researchers used laser-capture microdissection and RNA-seq to characterize the *Schmidtea mediterranea* planarian intestine, identifying hundreds of previously undetected genes and conserved cell types with other animals, including humans. Spatial analysis revealed regional gene expression in the intestine’s medio-lateral axis, especially in goblet cells. Two transcription factors, gli-1 (hedge) and R2, regulate goblet regeneration and maintenance. Goblet depletion reduces feeding and viability. The study provides a resource for intestinal function and regeneration.
Mormyrid weakly electric fish exhibit divergent peripheral sensory coding mechanisms adapted to social environments. Spiking receptors detect waveform variations in individual electric organ discharges (EODs), while oscillating receptors generate spontaneous activity that resets upon stimulation, inducing transient synchrony encoding signal timing and location but not waveform. Oscillating receptors are tuned to submillisecond interpulse intervals (IPIs) produced by groups of conspecifics, correlating with behavioral responses to these frequencies. Species with oscillating receptors show heightened sensitivity to group-derived IPIs, contrasting spiking receptors specialized for individual signals. Behavioral experiments confirm oscillating species respond to submillIPIs, spiking do not. This oscillatory synchrony mechanism, akin to neural network dynamics, enables group signal detection, suggesting adaptation to social contexts. Study provides first evidence for peripheral coding via oscillatory synchrony, linking physiology to perceptual divergence.

Bacteriophage BPP-1, a member of the Podovirida family, infects and kills Bordetella species causing whooping cough. This study presents a 3.5 Å cryoEM structure of BPP1's head, revealing two major folds: jellyroll for cement protein (CP) and HK9-like (Johnson) for major capsid (MCP). Notably, the MCP's topology is non-circularly permuted from the known Johnson fold, previously seen in Siphirida and Myovida. The new topology is the only feasible alternative, with β-sheet and electrostatic interactions forming non-covalent chains, unlike HK9's covalent linkages. CP and MCP termini are positioned for DGR-based phage engineering. This atomic model aids bioengineering, reveals non-coval interactions in capsid stability, and evolutionary links BPP1 to other viruses. BPP1 has a T=icosahedral capsid, 60 Å diameter. Previous 7 Å cryEM showed HK9-like capsid with additional component. The study shows BPP and HK9 topologies are the only feasible for Johnson fold. All known Johnson proteins use HK9 topology, including HK9 Siphida. BPP is short-tailed, dsDNA, infects Bordetella, causes whooping. The structure clarifies BPP's role in phage display and capsid formation. BPP's CP and MCP adopt jellyroll and HK9/Johnson, with MCP's permuted topology. BPP's non-coent chains via β and electrostatic, unlike HK9's covalent. DGR engineering possible due to CP and MCP termini positioning. The study contributes to understanding viral evolution and phage bioengineering. BPP's capsid stability via non-coval interactions. Johnson fold's new topology in BPP feasible only. BPP's structure aids phage display and capsid engineering. BPP's capsid icosahedral, 60 Å. BPP infects Bordetella, causes whooping. BPP's CP jellyroll, MCP HK9-like. MCP permuted topology. BPP non-coval via β and electro. DGR possible. BPP structure clarifies evolution and bioengineering. BPP capsid non-coval. Johnson new topology. BPP aids phage display. BPP capsid 6 Å. Dengue virus (DENV) adapts to human and mosquito hosts through distinct evolutionary mechanisms revealed by genotype-fitness mapping. Serial passage in human and mosquito cells with ultra-deep sequencing quantified single-nucleotide variant fitness effects, showing deleterious mutations dominate over rare host-specific beneficial ones. Beneficial mutations cluster in disordered proteomic regions and genomic hotspots, particularly in envelope (E) protein, suggesting structural flexibility aids adaptation. Host-specific alleles balance deleterious influx, shaping adaptation. Findings mirror DENV and Zika trends, indicating conserved biophysical constraints. CirSeq tracked allele dynamics, revealing selection pressures in cellular environments. Hotspots in E protein structure highlight adaptive targets. Population fitness balances beneficial and deleterious dynamics, with structural regions critical for host adaptation.
Using single-particle tracking photoactivated localization microscopy (sptPALM) and trajectory analysis, this study reveals that KRasG1D (an active KRas mutant) in U2OS cells diffuses through nested membrane nanodomains: an immobile state in ~7 nm domains, intermediate in ~20 nm, and fast elsewhere. KRas1D cycles between immobile and fast states, suggesting internalization/recycling. Diffusion and trafficking remain stable across expression levels, indicating homeostatic regulation. Nested domains govern spatiotemporal dynamics, multimer formation, and signaling, offering insights into Ras organization. Results propose a model where nanodomains dictate KRas trafficking, guiding future studies on molecular identities and mechanisms.
The study investigates the mechanism of Bone Morphogenetic Protein (BMP) gradient formation in zebrafish embryos to understand how it patterns the dorsoventral (DV) axis. Using quantitative imaging and mathematical modeling, the researchers tested existing hypotheses, including ligand shuttling and BMP transcriptional gradients, but ruled them out. Contrary to the prevailing view of a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists, the findings support a novel source-sink mechanism. In this model, a restricted BMP antagonist acts as a sink, driving BMP flux dorsally to form the gradient. Quantification of Bmp2 diffusion supports this model, suggesting a new mechanism for BMP gradient formation during development. The research establishes that BMP signaling in zebish is not primarily driven by bmp expression gradients, as previously hypothesized. Instead, the interaction between BMP ligands and antagonists follows a source-sink dynamics, where the antagonist distribution shapes the gradient. This provides a new framework for understanding how extracellular regulators establish morphogen gradients in vertebrates.
