High-resolution cryo-EM and X-ray structures reveal how kinesin motors generate force on microtubules via nucleotide-dependent conformational changes. Microtule binding triggers a conserved 'chpin' residue (N55) to open the nucleotide cleft, releasing ADP. ATP binding re-closes the cleft, coupling to translocation via the 'neck' linker, akin to myosin/F1-ATP mechanisms. Cryo-EM of human K49 in no-nucleotide and ADPAlFx states shows microtule-induced clamshell closure. N25 mutants exhibit microtule-attached rotational mobility, suggesting dimer coordination. These structures explain how microtules regulate kines nucleotide affinity, with implications for motor coordination and disease-linked uncoupling.
This study identifies a mitochondrial outer membrane quality control (MOMQC) pathway in *Saccharomyces cerevisiae* that degrades misfolded proteins via the ubiquitin-proteasome system. Temperature-sensitive outer membrane proteins Sen2-H and Sam5 are ubiquinated by Ubr1 and San1, respectively, requiring Hsp70 and Hsp4 chaperones. Degradation depends on Cdc8-N4-Ufd complex, Doa1, and Ubx. Substrates accumulate in mitochondria under proteasome impairment, distinct from ERAD. Pathway integrates cytosolic and mitochondrial components, revealing unique QC for homeostasis.
Researchers used a fluorescent resonance energy transfer (FRET) biosensor in transgenic mice to visualize extracellular-regulated kinase (ERK) activity in epidermal cells in vivo. Time-lapse imaging revealed focal ERK activation propagating radially from individual cells as transient "Spatial Propagation of Radial ERK Activity" (SPREAD), spreading up to 5 μm over3 min in basal and suprabasal layers. SPREAD frequency correlated with epidermal proliferation rates and was induced by 2-O-tetecanoylphol-3-acetate (PA) via epidermal growth factor receptor (EGR) and ligands. Wound healing triggered ERK waves parallel to the edge, indicating superimposed propagation. SPREAD coincided with G2/M cell cycle progression, linking ERK dynamics to division. SPREAD occurred in backskin epmis, suggesting general relevance. ERK, activated by GR-ligand binding, is critical for tissue homeostasis. The study reveals intercellular ERK propagation as a mechanism for synchronizing growth signals and proliferation in steady-state and wounded epidermis.
The study demonstrates that the cyanobacterial circadian clock aligns with midday across variable light-dark cycles, both in vivo and in vitro. The phase of the circadian rhythm scales linearly with day length, tracking midday regardless of photoperiod. Core Kai proteins, responsible for the oscillator, exhibit similar behavior when driven by metabolic pulses in vitro, indicating intrinsic dynamics. A mathematical model based on external cue-driven cycle transformation accurately predicts clock behavior diverse environments. The framework, with few parameters, quantitatively captures entrainment and guides molecular experiments. Cyanobacteria’s reconstit system (KaiABC) enables precise study, revealing seasonal adaptation mechanisms intrinsic Kai proteins. In vitro simulations with nucleotide pulses replicate in vivo scaling, confirming core mechanisms. Model predicts circadian responses time-varying conditions, applicable broadly. Findings suggest entrained behavior hinges on clock deformation by environmental coupling, simplifying prediction across organisms.
Loss of the transcription factor Meis1 in neural crest-derived sympathetic neurons disrupts sympatho-vagal cardiac regulation, causing chronotropic incompetence, conduction defects, and increased susceptibility to sudden cardiac death (SCD) in mice. Meis1 is critical for sympathetic target innervation, as its deficiency triggers apoptosis of sympathetic neurons from embryonic to perinatal stages and impairs Rab5+ endosome trafficking, essential for retrograde signaling and endosomal function. Meis operates independently of established transcriptional networks governing early sympathetic neuron specification, instead regulating later differentiation phases, including distal target innervation and trophic signaling. Genetic inactivation of Meis in rodents reveals imbalanced cardiac autonomic control, linking neurodevelopmental defects to adult SC. While Meis regulates neonatal cardiomyocyte mitosis (Mahmoud et al., 203), cardiomyocyte-specific Me1 alterations do not affect cardiac function, underscoring its role in neuronal, not cardiac, pathology. Findings position Me1 as a novel regulator of postmitotic sympathetic differentiation and endosomal machinery, with implications for unexplained SC.
This study demonstrates that mechanical instabilities and interfacial energy drive biofilm morphogenesis in *Vibrio cholerae*. Using mechanical measurements, simulations, image analysis, and mutagenesis, the authors show that growth-induced compressive stress triggers wrinkling and delamination, shaping biofilm surfaces. Interfac energy governs interface creation/annihilation, while feedback between mechanomorphogenesis and expansion dictates biofilm contour. These principles, conserved across organisms, suggest biofilms as a model for studying mechanical morphogenesis. Applications include engineering biofilm morphology via mechanical modulation.

The transcription factor neuron-restrictive silencer factor (NRS/REST) contributes to epileptogenesis by selectively repressing a subset of target genes in the hippocampus. Epilepsy-provoking seizures increase NRS levels severfold, yet only ∼1% of potential NRS target genes are repressed. These genes are rescued when NRS binding to chromatin is blocked. Selective repression occurs because these genes have mid-range NRS binding frequencies, making them sensitive to moderate NRS fluctuations. NRS-regulated genes include ion channels, receptors, and molecules critical to neuronal function, suggesting their repression contributes to epilepsy pathogenesis. Despite hundreds of hippocampal genes containing NRS response elements (NRSEs), only a few are repressed during epilept, as shown by rescue when NRS function is inhibited. Methods included transcriptome arrays, qPCR, ChIP, and gene set analysis. Key findings: (1) NRS regulates genes critical to neuronal function in epilept; (2) only a subset of NRSE-containing genes are repressed; (3 selectivity due to mid-range NRS binding. This dynamic regulation may influence neuronal properties in pathological and physiological contexts.
In the mammalian olfactory bulb, parallel processing of sensory information via mitral and tuft cells is driven by distinct lateral inhibitory circuits involving deep and superficial granule cells. Activity-dependent inhibition differentiates their responses, enabling odor discrimination across concentration ranges. Simulations suggest segregation of intensity. Similar to visual systems, connectivity differences in inputs create selectivity. Combined activity enhances encoding.
The study investigates allosteric modulation in monomers and oligomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor, a prototypical G-protein-coupled receptor (GPC), to elucidate molecular and supramolecular determinants of allostery. Using FRET-based conformational sensors, pharmacological assays, and molecular dynamics simulations, researchers found that monomers exhibit exclusively negative alloster effects driven by intramolecular electrostatic repulsion between alloster and orthosteric ligands. In contrast, oligomers display both positive and negative alloster effects, depending on ligand pairs, arising from interactions within and between protomers. Simulations revealed electrostatic, steric, and conformational determinants at atomic levels, with oligomeric behavior aligning with complex pharmacological profiles observed in myocardial preparations. Allosteric ligands like N-methylscopolamine, quinclidylbenilate, strychnine, and gallamine modulate receptor states (X_Y) differently in monomers vs. oligomers, highlighting oligom as critical for explaining multipic effects seen in native systems. Findings challenge monomer-centric models of allost, emphasizing GPC oligomerization’s role in functional diversity.
SETD, a histone methyltransferase that trimethylates H3K36, is essential for ATM activation following DNA double-strand breaks (DSBs) and homologous recombination repair by facilitating RAD5 filament formation. SETD-deficient cells, including clear cell carcinoma (ccR), exhibit impaired DNA signaling but fail to activate p5, a tumor suppressor rarely mutated in ccR, leading to reduced survival post-damage. This SETD-dependent chromatin modification primes cells for DSB repair. In ccR, SET loss may inactivate p checkpoint without TP5 mutations. Studies show SETD depletion reduces ATM, γH2AX, 5BP, and homology repair. Unlike other histone marks, SETD isn’t recruited to DSB but primes chromatin. This links chromatin alterations to genome integrity in cc, where SETD mutations co-occur with histone defects. Findings highlight SETD’s role in DDR and cancer. (2000 words)
A missense mutation in the Rasgrp1 EF-hand domain (RasgrpAnaef) in mice induces anti-nuclear autoantibodies and peripheral accumulation of CD4hi Helios+ PD-1+ CD4 T cells, dependent on B cells. Despite normal thymic development and TCR repertoires, RasAnaef causes tonic mTOR-S6 hyperactivation in naïve CD4 T, driving constitutive CD4 overexpression and effector cell expansion. mTOR inhibition in RasAnaef mice normalizes CD4 levels and abrogates autoantib, establishing mTOR as central to immune dysregulation. Unlike other autoimmune mutations (e.g., Ras1, Zap70), RasAna does not impair thym selection but reveals Ras1’s cell-intrinsic role in peripheral CD4 regulation. RasAna’s in vivo RasERK effects are milder than in vitro, highlighting context-dependent signaling. Ras1’s EF-hand mutation uncouples its thymic from peripheral functions, linking tonic mTOR to CD4 dysreg and autoimm. DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.0000009
Arida modulates Kras-dependent acinar cell identity changes in pancreatic tumorigenesis. In adult mice with oncogenic KrasG1D, inducible Arid1 suppression triggers irreversible acinar-to-ductal reprogramming into mucinous precursor lesions, while embryonic Ar1 silencing with Kras preserves acinar fate. Arid loss in adult acinar cells with Kras reduces chromatin accessibility at enhancers of acinar genes, enabling non-reversible metaplas and early neoplas. Alc blue confirms mucin retention in Arid-restored mice. Mechanistically, Arid stabilizes acinar identity via chromatin remodeling, acting as a barrier to Kras-driven neop, though other factors drive PanIN-PDAC progression. Timing and genetic context dictate SWI/SNF effects: acute Ar1 loss in Kras-mutant cells disrupts acinar function, enhancing sensitivity to oncogenic signals. Mouse models show context-specific neop modulation by Ar1, dependent on activation timing and Kras presence.
The study demonstrates sexually dimorphic effects of social isolation on paraventricular hypothalamic nucleus (PVN) corticotropin-releasing hormone (CRH) neurons. Brief isolation (<4 hr) of preadolescent female mice increased first spike latency (SL) and reduced excitability of CRH neurons, effects absent in age-matched males. Acute physical stress (forced swim) increased SL in both sexes, but prior isolation occluded stress effects in females. Glucocoid synthesis inhibition (metyrone) blocked isolation-induced SL increases, while exogenous corticosterone replicated them. CRH neuron excitability decreased with elevated SL. Findings indicate social isolation, not physical stress, elicits sex-specific PVN CRH responses: females interpret isolation as stress, males do not. Both sexes respond similarly to physical stress, but prior isolation negates subsequent stress effects in females. Hypothalamic slice recordings confirmed CRH intrinsic properties. Results suggest social context modulates neuroendocrine stress pathways sex-dependently.
The study investigates how hemoglobin (Hb) oxygen-binding affinity and cooperativity evolved to optimize tissue oxygen delivery in mammals. Using the Monod-Wym-Changeux (MWC) model, researchers mapped a "biophysical landscape" linking Hill coefficient (cooperativity) and conformational changes, revealing mammalian Hb resides on a ridge of maximum cooperativity, enabling fine-tuned oxygen unloading for tissue needs. Smaller mammals, with higher metabolic rates, exhibit lower Hb oxygen affinity but higher cooperativity, while larger mammals show the inverse, aligning with an inverse correlation between metabolic rate and Hb affinity. Evolutionary tuning of quaternary conformational states adjusts affinity-cooper ratios, maximizing cooperativity to meet tissue demands. Human data supports this, showing higher metabolic rates correlate with higher Hill values. This mechanism explains body-size adaptation in Hb function, balancing oxygen delivery efficiency across species. Findings highlight cooperativity as a key evolutionary strategy for physiological adaptation.
This study investigates the motility of Plasmodium sporozoites in the dermis and their interaction with blood vessels using intravital microscopy in mice. Sporozoites exhibit two distinct motility patterns: "avascular motility," characterized by high speed and minimal confinement in regions distant from vessels, and "perivascular motility," marked by constrained movement near blood vessels. Perivascular motility optimizes contact with dermal capillaries, facilitated by sporozoite track curvature. Mutant sporozoites lacking key adhesive proteins (CSN or TRAP-VAL) show impaired vessel entry: CSΔ sporozoites engage vessels longer but fail to invade, while TR-VAL sporozoites, with reduced gliding speed, explore less tissue and encounter vessels poorly. Sporozo motility adapts to environmental cues, transitioning from avascular to perivascular within 2 hours post-inoculation. Altered motility correlates with reduced infectivity, highlighting exit from the dermis as a critical barrier. These findings elucidate sporozo navigation strategies and identify motility as a target for intervention.
This study demonstrates that cells can adapt to the loss of essential components by inactivating other proteins, as shown in Saccharomyces cerevisiae evolved without BEM, a key polarity gene. After 0 generations, fitness reached9% of wild-type. Genomic analysis revealed reproducible mutations inactiv proteins restoring polarization. This shows evolutionary robustness to physi perturb and rapid divergence in functional modules. BEM’s newer role allowed ancient mechanisms. Adaptive steps improved speed. Inactivation over quantitative under selection.
This study investigates the neural mechanisms underlying economic commitment decisions involving long-term consequences, such as financial investments, using functional MRI. Participants evaluated prospects with sustained financial returns and chose to accept, reject, or defer decisions to a later time. The rostral medial prefrontal cortex (rmPFC) exhibited BOLD signals encoding stimulus value only during deferral, suggesting its role in integrating value over time, while the dorsal anterior cingulate cortex (ACC) encoded value during rejection or deferral. ACC activity revealed two decision biases: a tendency to defer commitments to later and greater weighting of losses over gains, which paradoxically maximized rewards in the task. The task involved irreversible, multi-alternative choices with prolonged financial ramifications, differing from prior studies on independent gambles. rmFC activity correlated with deferral, ACC with rejection. Behavioral and ACC data identified biases shaping decisions: delaying and prioritizing unfavored (excluded) options. Findings highlight distinct roles of medial prefrontal regions in value computation during commitment and deferral, offering insights into economic decision pressures and prefrontal cortex function.
Paraspeles, nuclear bodies containing the long non-coding RNA Neat1 and RNA-binding proteins, regulate gene expression by retaining mRNAs with 3′-UTR inverted Alu (IRu) repeats in the nucleus. In pituitary cells, paraspe components, including Neat1, NONO, and SFP, exhibit circadian expression. Inserting IRu into the 3′UT of EGFP cDNA induced rhythmic nuclear retention of egfp mRNA and circadian EGFP protein expression, which ceased when paraspeles were disrupted; antisense Alu had minimal effect. Real-time microscopy confirmed IRu-driven circadian EGFP expression. Disrupting parasles abolished rhythmicity in some circadian genes, implicating their role in post-transcriptional regulation. RNA-binding proteins like HNPC, which target antisense Alu, also show circadian patterns in pitary. These findings establish parasles as circadian regulators of gene expression via rhythmic mRNA retention, adding a post-transcriptional layer to circadian control.
The active-zone protein Munc3 regulates presynaptic voltage-gated calcium channel (VGCC) function, modulating calcium influx and recovery during high-frequency activity. Munc3 interacts with VGCCs via two basic residues in its C2 domain, as demonstrated by in vitro. Disrupting this interaction—via M3 deletion or a C2 mutant—alters VGCC response to action bursts and single-pulse calcium influx, revealing a novel synaptic modulation mechanism. M3 controls both vesicle priming and exocytosis, influencing information transfer at nerve terminals. VGCC-M3 interactions shape synaptic efficacy by adjusting calcium dynamics on millisecond-to-second timescales. M3 depletion (M3-KD) blocks exyt and reduces VGCC reactivation in 1.2 mM Ca²⁺, but not in rescued M3R/AA mutants, confirming the interaction’s direct role. Findings establish M3 as a key modulator of VGCC use-dependence, linking active-zone proteins to synaptic transmission control.
The study proposes that puzzle-shaped epidermal cells in plants, characterized by lobes and indentations, evolved to mitigate mechanical stress in isotropically growing tissues like leaves and cotyledons. When cells expand in two dimensions, turgor pressure (up to 1 bar in Arabidopsis) increases stress, risking rupture. Lobed shapes fragment cell areas, preventing large isodiametric cells that would bulge under pressure. Analysis of Arabidopsis cotyledon epidermis and simulations show isotropic growth correlates with lobe formation, while anisotropic tissues (stems, roots) lack them. Mutants with altered growth directions and 3D FEM models confirm lobes reduce stress. The mechanism suggests cells actively regulate shape to limit stress, with lobes enabling isotropic expansion without excessive strain. This links cell morphology to mechanical constraints of turgid cells in epidermis, demonstrating stress as a key driver of morphogenesis. Data across species and organs support the hypothesis, indicating puzzle shapes are adaptive in isodetric tissues but unnecessary in elongated ones.
Human pre-spliceosome assembly integrates cross-intron and cross-exon interactions to ensure accurate splicing of multi-intron pre-mRNAs. Fluorescent tagging of spliceosomal complexes in human extracts revealed that U1 binding to 5′ splice sites (5′SS) of adjacent introns synergistically accelerates U2 recruitment to the branch/3′ site of upstream introns, with flanking 5SS effects exceeding additive contributions. This synergy enhances U2 association, critical for multi-intron splicing efficiency. U1 binding to 5SS remains unaffected by downstream or upstream U2, indicating independent dynamics. Pre-spliceosomes form via two pathways: intron recognition via cross-intron (directly catalytic) or cross-exon (exon flanking exons first, then cross-exon interactions). Contrary to yeast, stable U2 requires prior U1 in humans. Downstream 5SS-bound U1 does not stabilize U2 once formed, but collaboration between flanking 5SS boosts U2 recruitment during pre-spliceosome formation. These mechanisms ensure exon definition and splicing accuracy.
This study identifies a ubiquitin-dependent protein-folding mechanism operating on the cytosolic side of the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) for membrane proteins, exemplified by the Wnt coreceptor LRP. Post-synthesis, LRP is ubiquinated at Lys-10, retaining it in the ER via interaction with a putative ubiquitin-binding chaperone, allowing folding. Successful folding is marked by palmitoylation and ER exit, facilitated by deubiquitination via USP9. Unsuccessful folding triggers polyubiquination of additional lysines, targeting LRP to ER-associated degradation (ERAD). This cycle mirrors the ER lumenal calnex/calreticulin system, where glucylation aids folding, while prolonged failure leads to de-mannosylation and ERAD. The mechanism extends to other poorly folding membrane proteins like CFTR and anthrax toxin receptor2 (CMG2), linked to cystic fibrosis and Hyal Fibromatosis, respectively. Lys-0-ubiquitination thus acts as a folding checkpoint, balancing retention and degradation, ensuring proteostasis. Key regulators include site-specific ubiquitination, USP9-mediated deubitination, and palmitoylation as exit signals. The system highlights ubiquitin’s role beyond degradation, in quality control of membrane protein folding, with implications for diseases involving misfolded proteins.
Sex differences in exploration strategies underlie similar decision-making performance in mice. In a two-armed bandit task, males explored more but persisted in exploration, while females explored less but learned faster. Computational models revealed sex-based differences in exploration and learning rates; females exhibited win-st behavior. These mechanisms may explain sex-based vulnerabilities in neuropsychiatric conditions like addiction/depression, emphasizing exploration as a translational target.
The study identifies Doa0 as a membrane-bound retrotransase in endoplasmic reticulum-associated degradation (ERAD), collaborating with the cytosolic ATPase Cdc8 to extract membrane proteins for proteasomal degradation. Using a reconstit system with *Saccharomyces cerevisiae* proteins, researchers demonstrated that Doa0, a ubiquitin ligase with four transmembrane segments, facilitates retrolocation by interacting with substrates’ transmembrane domains, enabling their passive transfer into the aqueous cytosol. Luminal domains cross the membrane in an unfolded state, with unfolding driven by Cdc8 action on the luminal side. This resolves how membrane-bound retrolocases and ATPases coordinate to extract proteins with folded luminal regions, addressing unresolved questions about retrolocation mechanisms. Doa’s role contrasts with other retrolocases like Derlin or Hrd, highlighting its specificity for ubiquitination- and Cdc8-dependent pathways. Findings suggest Do0’s membrane domain mediates TM segment delipidation and helix disruption, critical for multi-span protein extraction. ERAD parallels in other organelles (Golgi, mitochondria) also rely on ubiquination and Cdc8. The work establishes Doa as a core component of ERAD machinery, mechanistically linking ubiquitination, retrolocation, and unfolding, with implications for protein quality control beyond the ER.
Hedgehog (Hh) signaling regulates development and tissue homeostasis in in invertebrates and vertebrates, with dysregulation linked to developmental defects and cancers. This study investigates whether Drosophila Hh signaling requires proteolytic processing of Cubitus interruptus (Ci) into repressors. Using processing-resistant Ci variants expressed at physiological levels, researchers found that Ci variants (e.g., Ci-35, Ci-15) supported graded target gene activation and normal wing patterning in Drosophila imaginal discs when constitutive repressor suppressed ectopic dpp in anterior cells, demonstrating Hh can act as a morphogen without regulated Ci processing. Processing-resistant Ci variants were activated by Cos2 elimination or PKA inhibition, indicating Cos2 and PKA inhibit Ci via the CORD domain independently of processing. Third instar wing discs with Hh expressed in posterior cells signal to anterior cells at the anterior-posterior (AP) border, with Ci levels declining anteriorly. Animals lacking Fu and Su(fu kinases developed normal wings, though late discs lacked anterior En induction. Results show Hh patterning requires neither repressor nor full-length Ci level regulation by processing. Thus, Hh signaling in Drosophila can occur via Ci activation of full-length Ci without proteolysis, with Cos2 and PKA acting as inhibitors. Findings challenge the model that Ci processing is essential for Hh dose-dependent signaling, highlighting alternative regulatory mechanisms.
Calretinin (CR)-expressing neurons in the spinal dorsal horn form an excitatory networks that amplify pain signals and relay to lamina I projection neurons. Optogenetic activation of these interneurons in vivo elicited nociceptive behaviors, morphine-sensitive responses, conditioned aversion, and enhanced by nerve injury, while halorhodops inhibition raised sensory thresholds. CR neurons are interconnected, enabling them to modulate sensory input before relay to higher brain centers, as shown by photostimulation-induced nociception. Current models position CR neurons in polysynaptic circuits via SOM neurons to activate lamina I, consistent with CR-ChR photostimulation evoking EPSCs in superficial dorsal neurons. These findings expand roles of excitatory interne in linking innocuous to nociceptive circuits, suggesting CR networks are critical for pain amplification and potential analgesic targets.
This study investigates the role of Ras-associated protein 3 (RAB3) in osteogenesis, focusing on its regulatory mechanisms in cranial suture development. Mutations in *RAB3* cause Carpenter Syndrome, characterized by polysyndactyly and craniosynostosis. Rab3-deficient mice exhibit premature suture fusion due to aberrant osteoprogenitor proliferation and elevated osteogenesis, linked to upregulated *Fgf1*, *fgfrb*, and enhanced FGFR1-driven MAPK, Hedgehog, and RUNX2 signaling. RAB3 represses FGFR1-pERK/2 activity, directly inhibiting osteoprogen proliferation and RUNX-mediated differentiation while repressing GLI1, a mesenchym stem niche regulator. It also suppresses Hh signaling via canonical pathways and GLI through pK/2. Overlapping FGFR, Hh, and RAB3 mutations suggest shared mechanisms in intramembranous osteogenesis. In vitro pK2 inhibition normalized osteoprogen proliferation, corrected GLI/RUNX, and rescued suture fusion. RAB3 coordinates suture biogenesis via FG1-pK2-RUN2 and H-GL1 pathways, with GLI repression by p2. These findings establish RAB as a dual upstream regulator of FGFR and Hh, and non-canonically modulating GLI via p2 to control osteogenesis.
The study demonstrates that Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2)-mediated chromatin modifications at a gene can store epigenetic memory in cis (locally). Using ArabidopsisFLOWERING LOCUS C (FL), which is repressed by PRC during vernalization, researchers found that in plants with two FLC copies, one could be repressed while the other active. This mixed state persisted through cell divisions, indicating cis memory. Trans memory would require both copies ON/OFF. FLC repression is PRC-dependent, not methylation. Results show epetic stored in chromatin, not diffus factors. Demonstrates cis in Polycomb, similar to imprint/X-inactivation but without methylation. Chromatin modifications heritable.
This study investigates the structural and functional dynamics of BiP (Hsp70 chaperone) oligomers in the endoplasmic reticulum. Using site-specific proteolysis and crosslinking, researchers found that BiP oligomers form via adjacent protomers engaging the interdomain linker of one molecule in the substrate site of another, inactivating chaperone function. Native gel electrophoresis revealed a dynamic equilibrium between unfolded proteins and BiP oligomers, with lumenal calcium depletion inducing rapid oligomerization and reduced substrate binding, suggesting oligomers are inert. The equilibrium between inactive oligomers and active monomeric BiP buffers ER protein load fluctuations faster than covalent modifications or unfolded responses. BiP oligomerization, a storage form, competes with substrate binding to buffer ER stress. Calcium depletion shifts BiP to oligomers, favoring unfolded proteins over chaper. UPR induction and calcium depletion disrupt the unfolded-protein-oligomer correlation. Long-term stress may partition excess chaperones from UPR or heat shock responses. Feedback mechanisms complicate load-oligomer relationships. Findings suggest BiP oligomerization modulates ER homeostasis via rapid equilibration, with implications for stress responses.
This study presents a near-atomic cryoEM structure (3.9 Å) of Pepino mosaic virus (PepMV), a flexible filamentous plant virus, revealing homology between its coat protein (CP) and nucleoproteins (NPs) of Phleboviruses, enveloped -ssRNA animal viruses. Both bind and protect RNA, but differ in C-terminal regions: potexvirus CP forms helical rods, phlevo NPs form ribonucleoproteins. Structural similarity suggests evolutionary gene transfer between + and -ssRNA viruses. PepMV’s CP shares topology with Phlevo NP (e.g., RVV) when C-termini excluded, but divergent C-termini drive distinct assemblies. Potexviruses are non-enveloped, helical; phleviruses enveloped with icosahedral glycoprotein shells. High-res structures exist for Potexviruses (e.g., PapMV, BaMV), but not other flexuous families. Homology implies shared mechanisms for RNA protection despite divergent morphologies. Findings highlight convergent evolution in viral RNA encapsidation strategies. DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.195.05.
The study introduces SiteLoc, a method for nanoscale epitope localization on cellular surfaces using topological averaging to measure ligand-to-surface distances. Traditional epitope mapping is costly and unsuitable for high-throughput, while super-resolution techniques require complex preparation. SiteLoc achieves nanometer precision by averaging fluorescent signals along cell contours, calculating axial distances between ligand and reference channels. Validated on spherical and non-spherical cells, including bacteria, results align with prior data (e.g., Fc region on M protein). Applicable to heterogeneous surfaces and native morphology, enabling therapeutic antibody and vaccine development.
The study demonstrates that peripheral oxygen sensing in the skin, via HIF-1 and HIF-2 transcription factors, modulates systemic cardiovascular adaptation to hypoxia. In mice with epidermal HIF-1/H2 deficiency, 1% hypia altered blood pressure, heart rate, and temperature. The response involves initial HIF-1/NOS/NO vasodilation followed H2/Arg vasoconstriction. This first study shows keratin Hs role acute/chronic hyp, peripheral tissues systemic.
Researchers refined optogenetic methods to map synaptic connectivity at single-neuron resolution using temporal-focused two-photon excitation of soma-targeted channelrhodopsin (ChR). Restricting ChR to soma/proximal dendrites and expanding two-photon beams via temporal focusing enabled reliable action potential evocation in individual neurons, verified by GCaMPs, and synaptic connections via patch-clamp. Previous methods faced dendritic activation and fiber issues; this approach limits ChR to soma, reducing unintended activation. Enables precise circuit mapping for neural function.
The study challenges the assumption that Sox2 and Oct4 transcription factors assemble in an ordered manner on DNA, demonstrating instead revealing negative reciprocity in their interactions. Single-molecule data indicate that Sox2 enhances Oct4’s genomic binding while Oct4 reduces Sox2’s binding, contradicting prior interpretations of ordered assembly. Biophysical analysis suggests this reciprocity arises from either energy expenditure maintaining non-equilibrium states or mixed cooperativity (positive/negative) across genomic loci. The genomic-divers model posits that Sox2/Oct4 interactions vary between cooperative and antagonistic at different sites, with energy potentially sustaining non-equilibrium binding at some loci. Single-molecule methods detect this complexity, showing a single metric (Γ=-0.2) reflects mixed cooperativity across loci. Findings imply TF interactions are more dynamic than previously thought, with energy expenditure or locus-specific cooperativity driving reciprocity. This redefines understanding of TF-DNA dynamics, highlighting single-molecule approaches as tools to uncover hidden mechanisms. The work underscores the need for genomic-scale analysis to interpret reciprocity, with implications for transcriptional regulation complexity in living cells.
Drosophila melanogaster detect and avoid bacterial endotoxins lipopolysaccharides (LPS) through gustatory neurons expressing Gr6a bitter receptors, mediated by the chemosensory channel TRPA1. LPS aversion occurs during feeding and egg-laying, with TRPA expression in these neurons being necessary and sufficient for avoidance. TRPA activation by LPS is conserved, as LPS stimulates Drosophila neurons and activates human TRPA exogenously. TRPA-deficient mutants fail to avoid LPS-contaminated food, unlike controls. This mechanism may prevent Gram-negative infections, compensating for lack of adaptive immunity. TRPA’s role in LPS detection aligns with its function in nociception and noxious compound avoidance in Drosophila and mammals, suggesting a conserved sensory principle across species. Data indicate gustatory LPS detection via TR1 enables pathogen avoidance pre-infection.
Zinc finger protein Zfp35 is essential for the development of naïve T cells, as demonstrated in a mouse model with a hypomorphic mutation in Zfp35 (Zfp3bloto/bloto). These mice exhibit a deficiency in naïve T cells due to a developmental defect originating in the thymus and persisting in the periphery, affecting recent thymic emigrants. The defect is independent of thymic selection, proliferation, or Bcl-2 survival. Z35 binds gene promoters via a consensus motif, regulating genes involved in metabolism, mitochondrial function, and transcription. Reduced Z35 occupancy alters gene expression in thymocytes, with Ankle2 being a key target; restoring Ankle partially rescues maturation. An ENU-induced Z35 mutation causes impaired maturation in SP thymocytes and recent thym emigrants, linked to reduced viability. Z3 functions as a transcription factor critical for late thymic and post-thym maturation, with An2 dysregulation contributing to the defect. Findings establish Z3 as a key regulator of T cell maturation through transcriptional programs.
This study identifies mRNA:ncRNA interaction avoidance as a critical determinant of translational efficiency in prokaryotes, surpassing codon usage and mRNA structure in predicting protein abundance. Analysis of 7% species revealed reduced mRNA:ncRNA interactions compared to shuffled controls, with 3% phyla showing significant underrepresentation (p<0.05). Synthetically engineered GFP mRNAs with varying interaction potential demonstrated direct correlation between avoidance and expression levels, validating avoidance as a modulator of protein yield. While complete avoidance is combinatorially unfeasible due to abundant ncRNAs, selection for partial avoidance optimizes expression, balancing tradeoffs with other factors. Transcriptomic-proteomic meta-analyses confirm avoidance as robust predictor, comparable to codon bias and structure. Results establish evolutionary selection against stochastic mRNA:nc crosstalk, enabling precise protein engineering via avoidance design.
In a mouse model of amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS), longitudinal in vivo imaging of motor neurons and neuromuscular junctions (NMJs) reveals that NMJ denervation is a dynamic process of asynchronous dismantling and reinnervation, not sudden global motor neuron degeneration. In SOD1G7 mice, weeks before axonal degeneration, axonal branches are dismantled while new sprouting forms synapses at nearby NMJs. Denervation propagates from initially lost NMJs, with distal branches more susceptible, suggesting extrinsic neuromuscular factors drive degeneration. A prolonged window exists between early branch-specific damage and full axonal loss, potentially therapeutically targetable. Neuronal rescue in SOD mice fails to improve NMJ denervation, highlighting local branch-specific mechanisms. Early NMJ alterations in ALS include synaptic and molecular changes, with denervation occurring before motor neuron loss. Longitudinal analysis shows asynchronous NMJ loss within motor units, supporting local factors over global disease-driven degeneration. Findings challenge the "dying-back" hypothesis, emphasizing NMJ-specific dynamics as key to ALS progression.
This study demonstrates that estrogen receptor alpha (ERα) in the hypothalamus-preoptic area (hypothalam-PO) mediates physiological effects of tamoxifen in mice. Tamoxifen treatment induces changes in body temperature, bone density, and movement, mirroring human side effects like hot flashes and fatigue. Single-cell RNA sequencing reveals tamox alters gene expression in the hypoth-PO, with these changes dependent on ERα. Conditional ERα knockout in the-PO ablates tamox-induced gene expression and prevents temperature, bone, and movement changes. ER-deficient mice show no tamox side effects, confirming ERα’s role. Tamox acts as an ER antagonist in estrogen-sensitive regions, affecting thermoregulation and activity. The rodent model replicates human endocrine therapy side effects, highlighting ERα’s central role in tamox’s systemic impacts. Findings identify hypoth-PO ERα as a key mediator of tamox-induced physiology, offering insights for mitigating adverse effects.
ISG5, an interferon-stimulated ubiquitin-like protein, restricts *Listeria monocytogenes* in nonphagocytic cells via IFN-independently through STING-TBK1-IRF3/7 pathway. SILAC identifies ISGylation of ER/Golgi proteins enhancing cytokine secretion, countering infection. Demonstrates ISG5’s role in innate immunity against bacteria.
DeepRET is an open-source software that automates single-molecule Förster resonance energy transfer (smFRET) data analysis using deep learning, requiring only a user-defined quality threshold for raw image-to-histogram conversion. It integrates standard smFRET features to output kinetic metrics, achieving >95% accuracy on ground-truth data, surpassing human performance while using ~1% of the time. Trained on simulated/experimental data, it enables rapid, reproducible biomolecular dynamics quantification without manual preprocessing or rule-based analysis. The pre-trained deep neural network operates on Windows/Mac platforms, enabling non-programmers to generate publication-quality structural insights via automated classification, trace analysis, and visualization. DeepRET’s minimal intervention reduces human bias, demonstrated by confusion matrix validation of ground-truth vs. classified data, and enhances smRET reproducibility across labs. Its speed and objectivity support benchmarking dynamic studies in structural biology, protein folding, and super-resolution, advancing single-molecule research scalability.
SCML2, a mammalian homolog of Drosophila Polycomb-group protein SCM, interacts with Polycomb repressive complex 1 (PRC1) via its SPM domain and binds noncoding RNAs (ncRNAs) through a novel RNA-binding region (RBR). SCMLA, a chromatin-associated isoform of SC2, recruits PRC to target genes and represses transcription, requiring coordinated action of MBT domains, RNA binding, and PRC interaction. Deletion of RBR reduces SCMLA occupancy at genes and impairs PRC recruitment, indicating ncAs regulate SCML function. SCML directly represses some genes independently of PRC. In Drosophila, SCM-PRC interaction relies on SPM, conserved in humans. RBR and MBT domains recruit SCML to chromatin, where it stabilizes PRC. Mutant SCML lacking RBR causes PRC release. SCML-bound ncAs identified in vivo. SCML bridges ncAs and epigenetic regulation, contributing to PRC-dependent and -independent repression, highlighting combinatorial factors in Polycomb complex specificity.
This study investigates the molecular mechanisms by which the SecM peptide stalls ribosomes during translation in *Escherichia coli*, regulating the SecA-dependent secretion pathway. Using cryo-EM, the authors resolved two structures of SecM-stalled ribosomes at 3.3–3.7 Å resolution, revealing two distinct stalling mechanisms at different elongation steps. One mechanism involves inactivation of the peptidyl-transfer center (PTC), inhibiting bond formation with incoming proly-tRNA; the other involves prolonged residence of peid-RNA at the hybrid/P site, inhibiting tRNA translocation. Structural analysis shows interactions between SecM and the ribosomal tunnel induce conformational changes in the PTC, leading to arrest at elongation steps. The SecM-Gly-RNC conformation locks the PTC in an inactive state, preventing stable A-site tRNA binding. The tRNA-nas peptide position in SecM-RNA resembles active PTC ribosomes, suggesting an alternative stalling mechanism. These findings demonstrate how SecM modulates translation through dynamic reshaping the ribosome’s functional centers, offering insights into translational control by regulatory peptides.
This study investigates foveal cone photoreceptor density in relation to axial eye length using an Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope in 8 eyes of6 subjects. Cone density was measured in visual angle (cones/deg²) and retinal units (cones/mm²). Longer eyes exhibit expanded foveal mosaics, but not proportionally to axial length. Retinal stretching reduces cones/mm, yet myopes show higher angular density (cones/deg) near fove compared to emmetropes, suggesting enhanced visual acuity potential. Deficits in best-corrected fove vision in myopes cannot be attributed to photoreceptor spacing from retinal stretching. Adaptive optics enables high-resolution fove imaging, revealing prior studies overlooked fove center. Myopes maintain good acuity with correction despite structural changes. Findings challenge assumptions linking myopic deficits to retinal stretching, highlighting angular density as critical for visual performance.
This study identifies genetic factors in *Streptococcus pneumoniae* influencing asymptomatic carriage duration beyond serotype. Using longitudinal swab data and whole-genome sequencing, pneumococcal variation explained 6% of duration variability, host traits (age, prior carriage) <5%. Heritability partitioning revealed serotype (7%), drug-resistance (9%), and other loci (7%) as major contributors. A panome-wide study linked prophage sequences to reduced duration, possibly via competence disruption, independent of serotype. Findings align with theoretical models of pneumoc competition and resistance. Non-serotype variants exist in natural populations, with loci identified, linking duration to resistance. Despite host factors, pneumoc genome dominates variability. Phage effects imply diversification via infection/loss during carriage. *S. pneumoniae* genetics drive duration; environmental factors (e.g., host status) may alter heritability. Lower heritability bounds estimated via SNPs.
The study investigates the stoichiometry of the KCNE1 and KCNQ1 subunits in the cardiac IKs ion channel complex using engineered fusion proteins and photo-crosslinking techniques. By linking KCNE1 to one, two, or four KCNQ subunits, researchers created 4:4, 2:4, and1:4 stoichiometries. Whole-cell and single-channel recordings showed that higher β-subunit numbers caused hyperpolarized activation, reduced conductance, and shorter opening latency. UV-crossing with an unnatural amino acid in KCNE1 revealed progressive slowing in crosslinking rates with more KCQ, indicating no intrinsic limit to four β-subunit association. The findings support a variable KCNE: KCN1 stoichiometry up to4:4, suggesting flexibility in β-subunit association modulates cardiac repolarization.
This study investigates the role of interleukin-1β (Il-1β) in zebrafish fin regeneration, revealing that a transient inflammatory response mediated by Il-1β is essential for proper tissue regeneration. Epithelial cells, not myeloid cells as previously thought, are the primary source of Il-1β following injury, initiating the inflammatory response. Macrophages resolve this inflammation by attenuating Il-1 expression, which is crucial for regenerative cell survival. Prolonged Il-1 signaling leads to apoptosis of regenerative cells, as seen in myel-defective mutants. Il-1 is necessary for activating regeneration-induced genes, highlighting its dual role: essential for regeneration but harmful in excess. Macrophages regulate Il-1 levels, balancing inflammation to ensure successful regeneration. The study shows that myoid cells, particularly macrophages, are critical in regeneration, influencing tissue patterning and growth. Findings indicate that controlled Il-β signaling, modulated by macroph, is vital for regeneration. Excessive Il-β causes apoptosis, as observed in mutants lacking myel cells. Thus, Il-β and inflammation act as a double-edged sword: necessary for regeneration but harmful in excess. The study elucidates macroph’s role in resolving inflammation, a first identified function in this context. Overall, proper Il-β levels and inflammatory response, regulated by macroph, are essential for regeneration progression.
Protein diffusion in the cytoplasm of Escherichia coli, Lactoccus lactis, and Halofer volcanii depends on surface charge and ionic strength. In E. coli, positively charged proteins diffuse up to 10-fold slower due ribosome interactions; L. lact/Hfx less due electrostatic screening. Ribosome binding (non-selected, µM) limits proteome. Prokaryotic proteomes have fewer positive proteins. Paradox in symbionts.
The study identifies Smed-follatin, a follistatin homolog in planarians, as critical for initiating regeneration following tissue absence. Smed-follatin inhibits Activin signaling (via Smed-activin-1 and Smed-activ-2) to trigger regenerative responses specifically when tissue is missing, not during normal turnover. Wound-induced Smed-follatin expression correlates with injury severity, persisting longer after major tissue loss than minor injuries. Inhibition of Smed-follatin blocks tissue-specific regeneration (e.g., mitotic/apoptotic responses) but not all wound reactions (e.g., neoblast migration). TGF-β signaling, central to regeneration, is modulated by this follatin-activin axis. Planarians distinguish injury types via Smedoll-mediated signals, revealing a conserved mechanism for regeneration initiation.
This study characterizes the mutational landscape of mismatch repair (MMR)-deficient tumors using whole-genome and whole-exome sequencing of 52 tumor-normal pairs (3 MMR-deficient). MMR-def tumors exhibit a distinct mutation signature resembling germline DNA, with 25-fold higher somatic events (21 vs9 per tumor) and indels enriched in homopolymer repeats, enabling microsatellite instability (MSI) detection. A novel indel-based marker outperformed standard MSI tests in specificity for endometrial MMR-def tumors. Recurrent indels overlapped "double-strand break (SB) repair by homologous recombination" pathways, correlating with reduced SB repair capacity in MMR-def cells. MMR-def tumor cultures showed dose-dependent sensitivity to SB inducers. Sequencing validated 8% substitutions and6% indels. Findings suggest MMR deficiency links replication errors to germl variation and highlights SB-targeted therapies for M-def cancers.
Researchers optimized Vivid-derived "enhanced Magnets" (eM) for subcellular optogenetics, eliminating concatemer and 8°C preinc requirements. eM enable rapid, reversible protein recruitment to organelles, induce contacts, and reconstit OSBP-VAP ER-Gol tethers for lipid transport. With improved thermal stability and kinetics, they allow precise spatiotemp control. Structure-guid redesign enhanced dimer efficiency, validated in assays. eM offer low background, ideal for small volumes and organel manipulation.
Ribosome stalling during translation triggers neurodegeneration, but the underlying signaling pathways remain unclear. In C5BL6-Gtp2nm25/- mice, where elongation is stalled at AGA codons due to tRNA and GBP2 deficiencies, GCN2 kinase activates eIFα phosphorylation and ATF4, initiating the integrated stress response (ISR) independently of tRNA acylation. This occurs prior to neurodegeneration in cerebellum and hippocampus, with ISR attenuating neuronal loss. GCN deletion exacerbates degeneration, highlighting its protective role. ATF4 activation persists even without tRNA, suggesting GCN may respond to stalled ribosomes broadly, as in amino acid deprivation. Findings reveal ribosome stalling links elongation to initiation via GCN, critical for neuronal survival under stress.
A century analysis of adult height trends in 20 countries (8.6 million participants) reveals South Korean women (2.2 cm) and Iranian men (6.5) gained most; sub-Saharan and South Asia had little change. Dutch men (8.5) are tallest; Guatem women (4.3) shortest. The tallest-shortest gap remained stable for women but widened for men, with no convergence.
Opioids induce respiratory depression by targeting two brainstem sites, primarily the preBötzinger Complex (preBötC), where breathing rhythm originates. In mice, opioid-induced respiratory depression (OIRD) is mediated by ~7–10 glutamatergic neurons in the preBöC expressing µ-opioid receptors (Opr1). Deletion of O1 in these neurons eliminates IRD, even at high fentanyl doses, without affecting analgesia. Genetic tools show 7–10 preBöC neurons suffice to rescue IRD, with Dbx1/Foxp2 subtypes critical. O1 broadly expressed in CNS/ PNS, but preBöC sites dominate IRD. Remaining opioid effects may stem from incomplete receptor deletion or minor sites. Targeting these neurons could yield safer opioids or overdose therapies.
The study identifies sodium taurocholate cotransporting polypeptide (NTCP), a liver-expressed transmembrane transporter, as a functional receptor for human hepatitis B virus (HBV) and hepatitis D virus (HDV). Using near-zero distance photo-crosslinking and tandem purification, researchers showed that the pre-S domain of HBV's large envelope protein interacts with NTCP. Silencing NTCP blocked HBV and HDV infection, while its exogenous expression enabled nonsusceptible hepatocarcinoma cells to become susceptible. Replacing amino acids 171–6 of nonfunctional monkey NT with human NT restored viral infection support. The N-terminal myristoylated peptide of pre-S24–28 effectively blocked HBV and HDV by engaging an unknown receptor, likely NTCP. Biochemical and virological assays confirmed NTCP's role as a common receptor for both viruses, with residues 17–6 critical for pre-S binding and infection. These findings establish NTCP as essential for HBV and HDV entry.
Sepsis induces persistent alterations in memory CD8 T cell phenotype and function, impairing immune responses to reinfection. In septic patients and murine models (CLP-induced seps), central memory CD8 T cells (TCM) proliferate more than effector memory (TEM) post-seps-induced lymphopenia, driving recovery of pathogen-specific CD8 cells but altering compartment composition and tissue localization. Transcriptional and chromatin changes in seps survivors indicate long-term intrinsic T cell modifications, reducing capacity to control L. monocytogen. P48 T cell studies show numeric recovery via homeostatic proliferation, with TCM overrepresentation skewing memory pools. Functional deficits include impaired TCR adhesion/synapse formation >30 days post-seps. Seps accelerates memory T compartment aging, biasing toward TCM, compromising long-term immunity.
APOEC3A, a member of the APOBEC3 family of cytidine deaminases, inhibits LINE-1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-str DNA during integration. LINE-1 retrotrans, a mutagenic process in human genomes, is regulated by cellular mechanisms including APOEC3 enzymes, which convert cytidines to uridines in single-str DNA. APO3A is the most potent inhibitor, yet prior studies failed to detect deamination in retrotrans events, leaving its mechanism unclear. Using in vitro and in vivo assays, including the L1 Element Amplification Protocol (LEAP), researchers demonstrated that A3 deaminates single-str DNA transiently exposed during L1 integration. Engineered L1 retrotrans in cells coexpressing A3 and uracil glycosylase inhibitor (UGI) revealed strand-specific C-U deamination, confirming A3 activity. Overexpression of wild-type A3 (but not deamin-defective mutants) inhibits L1, but prior studies missed deamination due to degradation of deaminated intermediates, reducing retrotrans and erasing evidence. Deamination in pre-integration sites leads to inexact target site duplications post-integration. Stable UGI expression reversed A3-mediated inhibition, showing U removal of uracil residues rescues retrotrans. These findings establish that A3 inhibits L1 by deamin transient single-str DNA during integration, explaining its antiretrotrans activity despite prior lack of detectable deamination in retrotrans products.
This study investigates the role of Rem2, an activity-regulated non-canonical Ras-like GTPase, in visual circuit plasticity and neuronal function. Rem2-deficient (Rem2/-) mice fail to exhibit normal ocular dominance (OD) plasticity during the critical period, indicating a role in experience-dependent plasticity. At the cellular level, Rem2 regulates intrinsic excitability of layer2/3 pyramidal neurons independently of synaptic changes. In vitro and in vivo recordings show increased spontaneous firing in Rem2 absence. Rem2 stabilizes neuronal excitability and circuit function in response to sensory changes. Rem2 expression is acutely regulated by visual experience, suggesting a role in activity-dependent processes following sensory deprivation. Findings show Rem2 functions as a regulator of plasticity at morphological, cellular, and circuit levels, linking molecular regulators of neuronal morphology to broader function. Rem2 may regulate cortical responsiveness to visual stimulation via neuronal excitability or synaptic scaling. The study identifies Rem2 as a key molecule at the nexus of a network sensing and responding to neuronal activity to preserve circuit function. Defining Rem2 mechanisms will elucidate activity-dependent circuit modifications.
The article "Physical limits magnetogenetics" critically evaluates the feasibility of using magnetic fields to control biological systems, addressing recent claims of magnetosensation and magnetetics. Qin et al. (206) proposed a protein acting as a magnetic compass for animal orientation, while Stanley et.05 and Wheeler et.6 reported magnetic control of membrane conductance via ferritin-ion channel complexes. The author argues these claims conflict with physics, noting discrepancies up to 5-1 log units in forces/torques/temperatures needed for effects. Ferritin’s paramagnetism limits utility, as forces are too small for molecular orientation or channel effects. If phenomena occur, mechanisms differ from proposed. Experiments with TRPV-ferritin fusion show weak evidence (8/200 cells responded). Magnetosensation theories include magnetite-based (Kirschvink) or biochemical (Ritz). Magnetetics via ferritin manipulation lacks viability without new physics. Renewed interest in magnetic effects faces unresolved biophysical challenges.
Transient nuclear Prospero expression in *Drosophila* neural progenitors (neuroblasts) induces quiescence. Low nuclear Prospero levels transiently precede quiescence entry, distinct from self-renewal (absent Prospero) and differentiation (high Prospero). Loss of Prospero blocks quies, while inducing low Prospero in proliferating neurobl triggers quies. Temporal factors alter quies timing but not Prospero pulse correlation. Prospero distinguishes progenitor fates: absent (self-renew), low (quies), high (differentiation). Drosophila neurobl model reveals Prospero’s role in quies regulation. (206 words)
Researchers developed a single-particle fluorescence assay to track HIV-1 capsid uncoating in vitro after membrane permeabilization. Uncoating initiates with a rate-limiting first defect, followed by rapid collapse. PF7 accelerates opening but stabilizes the lattice; polion delays initiation but not disassembly. Capsids are structurally heterogeneous, many with defects causing immediate unco. Single-molecule analysis reveals steps masked in ensemble. Bind differentially regulate stages, interplay controls kinetics. Offers antiviral targets.
The study investigates the role of the c-Jun N-terminal kinase (JNK) signaling pathway in breast cancer development. Analysis of human breast cancer genomes reveals frequent loss-of-function mutations in the JNK pathway, which is unexpected since JNK typically promotes cell proliferation via the AP1 transcription factor. Experiments in mice show that loss of JNK in mammary epithelial cells leads to genomic instability and breast cancer. JNK deficiency also accelerates tumor formation in a murine breast cancer model, associated with early neoplas and genomic instability. The tumor suppressive role of JNK is not in growth of established tumors but in preventing initiation. These findings suggest JNK pathway defects are driver mutations that promote tumor initiation. The study demonstrates that JNK loss causes murine breast cancer and accelerates tumor development in models. Frequent JNK mutations in human breast cancer may be driver mutations promoting development. JNK plays a key role in early stages of cancer by promoting mammary neplas. The study clarifies the significance of JNK loss in cancer, showing it is not passenger but driver. Major conclusions: JNK deficiency in mammary epithelium causes murine cancer and accelerates tumor development in models.
A bioactive amidating enzyme, peptidylglyine α-amidating monooxygenase (PAM), is essential for ciliogenesis across species, including *Chlamydomonas reinhardtii*, planaria, and mice. PAM deficiency disrupts motile and primary cilia assembly, with *Chlamydomonas* knockdowns failing to form cilia beyond the transition zone and exhibiting altered Golgi architecture. Planaria with reduced PAM show decreased ventral ciliary density and cytoplasmic axonemes lacking membranes. Mammalian neuroepithelial cells in *Pam/-* embryos display aberrant primary cilia. PAM’s amidation and post-Golgi trafficking roles contribute to ciliogenesis, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved link between amidation and ciliary assembly. While *Chydomonas* and planaria show severe ciliary defects, mice exhibit shorter cilia rather than complete loss, indicating tissue-specific sensitivity. These findings highlight PAM as a conserved regulator of early ciliogenesis, bridging amidation and membrane trafficking in ciliary biogenesis.
This study investigates the interaction between class III myosins (Myo3) and the actin-bundling protein Espin, which are essential for the development and maintenance of stereocilia in vertebrate hair cells. My3 interacts with Espin through its tail homology I (THDI) motif, but the mechanism by which My3 regulates Espin's actin assembly and stabilization was unclear. The research reveals that My3's THDI contains two repeat sequences that independently bind Espin's ankyrin repeats, enabling My3 to cross-link Espin. Structural analysis of the My3-Espin complex elucidates the binding mechanism and shows My3 can release Espin's auto-inhibition. Evidence suggests My3 cross-linking enhances Espin's actin bundling activity, increasing bundle diameter. My3 and Espin co-localize at stereocilia tips, and their co-expression promotes stereocilia elongation in cultured hair cells. My3 transports Espin to actin structures like filopodia and stereilia, where Espin's WH domain aids protrusion elongation. My3's motor domain affinity for actin may enhance bundling in full-length My3-Espin complexes. The study identifies a novel mechanism by which myosin binding regulates actin bundling via an actin crosser, highlighting My3's role in promoting parallel actin bundle formation in stereilia.
Voltage-gated calcium channels (CaV) regulate cellular functions such as muscle contraction and neurotransmission by translating membrane depolarization into calcium influx. This study investigates the structural and energetic mechanisms governing voltage-sensing domains (VSDs) in the eukaryotic CaV1.1.1 channel using computational modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophys. Two functionally distinct VSDs in Ca1.1.1 adopt the sliding-helix model but differ in ion-pair interactions at outer gating charges. Stabilization of activated states enhances voltage dependence, while resting-state stabilization slows kinetics. Structural models of activated and resting VSDs, derived from cryo-EM and Rosetta homology, reveal these ion-p differences underpin distinct gating properties. Experimental validation via mutagenesis confirmed predicted effects, aligning with computational predictions. Findings establish a mechanistic link between ion-p formation in VSDs and voltage-gated channel gating, clarifying how structural asymmetry enables diverse physiological roles.
This study investigates the role of matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) in visual plasticity in the adult healthy and stroke-affected brain. Findings indicate that an optimal level of MMP-activity is essential for visual plasticity to occur. In healthy mice, 7-day application of MMP-inhibitor prevented visual plasticity, while a single treatment after stroke rescued plasticity, but two treatments diminished it. These results suggest that upregulation of MMP shortly after stroke impairs plasticity, which can be rescued by brief inhibition. The study shows that precise regulation of MMP-activity is crucial for plasticity in the adult brain and offers potential for recovery after stroke. Using in vivo imaging and behavioral tests, the research demonstrates that MMPs are vital for visual cortex plasticity, particularly in increasing open eye responses after monocular deprivation (MD). Under normal conditions, MMP supports brain function, but in disease, increased activity can impair recovery. Previous studies show increased MMP after stroke and partial rescue with inhibition. This study confirms that stroke-induced impairment of visual plasticity can be rescued by post-stroke MMP inhibition. Together, the data highlight a critical role of MMPs in adult plasticity and suggest that their activity must be within a narrow window for experience-induced plasticity to occur.
AMPA receptors, tetrameric ligand-gated ion channels critical for excitatory synaptic transmission, assemble as dimer-of-dimers with extracellular amino-terminal (ATD) and ligand-binding (LBD) domains. Cryo-EM structures reveal potential lateral movements during gating, but functional studies using bifunctional methanethiosulfate cross-linkers show that extracellular domains adopt stable "opened" conformations upon prolonged glutamate exposure. However, the auxiliary subunit Stargaz restricts this flexibility, stabilizing compact conformations. Under synaptic conditions—brief glutamate transients and auxiliary presence—AMPA receptors maintain compact extracellular domains during activation and desensitization. Long glutamate exposure or absence of auxiliars permits structural rearrangements. Thus, auxili like Staz constrain conformational dynamics, ensuring synaptic fidelity.
Researchers developed an optimized intestinal digestion protocol to isolate immune cells from helminth-infected murine intestines, overcoming challenges posed by type 2 immune responses that drive mucus production, tissue remodeling, and immune infiltration. Using high-dimensional spectral flow cytometry and confocal microscopy, they validated the protocol’s accuracy, identifying immune hallmarks of anti-paras responses across infection stages. The method enables detailed single-cell analysis of local immune dynamics previously unfeasible. Validation via flow and microscopy confirmed cell ratios, showing local changes. Emphasizes need for effective isolation to study intestinal helth responses directly. References prior studies on mucus, epithelium, infiltration.
This study employs an integrated experimental and computational approach to elucidate the dynamic regulation of 20S proteasomes, revealing that substrate transport into the proteasome is the rate-limiting step for hydrolysis. Using fluorogenic peptides and kinetic assays, the authors demonstrate that substrate/product interactions with proteolytic and regulatory sites modulate catalytic activity over time, with differences between standard and immuno-proteas affecting degradation rates and cleavage-site usage. The model, validated across purified and homogen systems, captures core proteasome dynamics, suggesting applicability in cellular contexts. Findings highlight transport efficiency as a key determinant of isoform-specific proteolysis, advancing understanding of proteas regulation in protein homeostasis and immunity.
The β-alanine transporter Bala is essential for visual transmission in *Drosophila* by recycling β-alanine, a byproduct of histamine metabolism. Histamine, critical for synaptic signaling, is rapidly taken up by lamina glia, converted to carcinine, and returned to photoreceptors where it is hydrolyzed to histamine and β-alanine. While histamine is repackaged into vesicles, β-alanine’s return to glia was unclear. This study identifies Bala, a β-al transporter in retinal pigment cells. *Bala* mutants show reduced β-alanine levels, retinal accumulation deficits, impaired synaptic activity, and phototaxis. Bala’s expression in pigment cells is vital for visual transmission, revealing their role in neurotransmission. β-al recycling sustains histamine homeostasis and synaptic function. Retinal pigment cells store β-al, likely via Bala, compensating for its loss during histamine-to-carcinine conversion. Disrupting Bala disrupts β-al transport, causing synaptic failure. The pathway involves carcinine synthesis in lamina glia, transport to photoreceptors, hydrolysis, and Bala-mediated β-al recycling. Findings establish a conserved β-al role in *Drosophila* and mammals, completing the histamine cycle critical for vision. This clarifies retinal pigment cells’ role and β-al trafficking in sustaining photoreceptor transmission.
Chronologically aging *Saccharomyces cerevisiae* cells undergo a redox collapse affecting ~80% of thiol-containing proteins days before death, driven by early NADPH depletion rather than increased oxidants, contradicting the free radical theory of aging. NADPH decline initiates a cascade downregulating cellular processes; caloric restriction extends lifespan by elevating NAD and delaying this cascade. The redox collapse, observed in cytosolic, nuclear, and mitochondrial proteins, mirrors metabolic shifts in other organisms, suggesting conserved mechanisms. The study establishes a timeline of proteome oxidation preceding death in postmitotic aging, highlighting NAD as a central regulator of redox homeostasis.
The study elucidates the mechanism by which the dimeric D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase (DTD) from *Plasmodium falciparum* enforces homochirality during translation by selectively removing D-amino acids erroneously charged onto tRNAs. The crystal structure of DTD with a substrate-mimicking analog reveals a conserved cross-unit Gly-Pro dipeptide motif that captures the chiral center of D-aminoacyl-tRNA, enabling side-independent recognition of diverse D-amino acids without direct catalytic residues. This mechanism allows DTD to act on multiple D-amino substrates while sparing L-aminoacy-tRNAs, which evade proofreading due to steric clashes in the active site. The cross-unit interactions, particularly the Gly-Pro motif from both monomers, stabilize the D-amino moiety via hydrogen bonds, ensuring strict chiral specificity. DTD operates in trans, likely recapturing aminoacy-tRNAs from EF-Tu, preventing D-amino incorporation into proteins. Earlier models based on apo structures or non-cognate ligands lacked mechanistic clarity, but this study clarifies how DTD’s architecture enforces enantioselective proofreading, a critical for maintaining homochir in the translational apparatus. The findings highlight a universal strategy for chiral enforcement, leveraging weak hydrogen bonds and geometric constraints to exclude L-amino, ensuring fidelity in protein synthesis. DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.1119.0401.754.
The cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) is an ATP-binding cassette (ABC) protein that functions as a chloride channel regulated by ATP and phosphorylation. Normally, ATP binding induces dimerization of two nucleotide-binding domains (NBDs), opening the pore, while hydrolysis disrupts the dimer and closes it. Spontaneous openings without ATP are rare in wild-type CFTR but occur in some mutants and are enhanced by ivacaftor, a CFTR potentiator. This study shows that even spontaneous openings are coupled to NBD dimerization, which can occur without ATP. Thus, coordinated NBD and pore movements are intrinsic to CFTR, with ATP altering stability but not structure of open/closed conformations. This explains effects of phosphorylation, mutations, and drugs on both ATP-driven and spontaneous activity. ATP acts as a 'molecular glue' stabilizing the transition and open states, not changing global conformations. The absence of ATP binding in open channels prevents defining open-state affinity, making gating follow a linear sequence rather than detailed balance cycle. Findings constrain models of ATP-dependent gating and modulatory effects, relevant to ABC proteins. Low-probability ATP-independent openings in a CFTR lacking NBD2 are not linked to dimer, but NBD-TMD coupling upon opening mimics NBD dimerization. This provides insights into CF mutations and drug mechanisms.
Researchers propose a mechanism where transmembrane proteins assemble in lipid bilayers via pre-transition effects near order-disorder phase transitions. Simulations show proteins with hydrophobic thickness matching disordered phase in ordered bilayers create interfaces; proximity reduces interfac energy when assemble, termed "orderphobic" effect. Strength depends phase proximity and size/mismatch. Forces exceed membrane elasticity. At 24K DPP, 2.6nm induce disorder. Dual: disordered proteins nucleate order. Interfaces drive assembly. Hydroph ordered>dis. Single-molecule tests possible. Biological more complex.
The study investigates the relationship between action potentials (APs) and calcium fluorescence signals in layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons of the visual cortex of GCa6 transgenic mice using in vivo. Simultaneous calcium imaging and cell-attached recordings revealed that while most APs are detectable under optimal conditions (soma filling the field of view), typical population imaging conditions (large field, many neurons) yield low detection rates: <1% for single APs and 2–3% for doublets. AP-calcium transfer functions were constructed by isolating events with specific inter-spike intervals (GC6s: ≥00/50 ms; GCf: ≥30 ms), but only a minority of APs contributed. Summed APs over 0 ms showed limited detection under realistic imaging. Spike inference models tested under optimal (soma-focused) and suboptimal (population) conditions highlight challenges in interpreting calcium data. Findings underscore limitations of GCa indicators in population studies and inform analysis of datasets like the Allen Brain Observatory. Results emphasize the need for caution in inferring spiking activity from calcium signals in large-scale imaging.
Females of the cave-dwelling insect *Neotrogla* possess a novel genital valve mechanism enabling receipt of multiple seminal packages during prolonged copulation. The valve, composed of two plate-like sclerites and canals, directs seminal flow to one of two insemination slots, allowing a second capsule to be received while the first is being consumed. This structure, paired with a spiny, penis-like intromittent organ that coercively anchors males during 4–7-hour copulations, facilitates female competition for nutrient-rich seminal gifts in nutrient-poor habitats. Seminal capsules serve both fertilization and nutrition, with formation requiring ~5 minutes in related species but extended in *Neotla* due to prolonged mating. The valve’s evolution may underpin the reversal of intromitt organ roles, where females compete for males to obtain seminal resources, contrasting typical male-driven sexual selection. This morphological innovation supports female-female competition for nuptial gifts, a key driver of reversed genital evolution in the genus. Related species lacking such structures exhibit shorter copulations, highlighting *Neotla*’sexaptation to oligotrophic environments through dual seminal acquisition. The valve’s dual slots and flow control refine understanding of sexual role reversal, linking anatomy to resource competition in extreme habitats.
This study elucidates the role of Munc18 in regulating stage-wise SNARE complex assembly during synaptic exocytosis. Using optical tweezers, researchers identified four distinct stages of SNARE assembly involving N-terminal (NTD), middle (MD), C-terminal (CTD), and linker domains (LD). NTD and CTD mediate vesicle docking and fusion, respectively, while MD regulates assembly and fusion. Munc8 initiates SNARE assembly independently of syntaxin’s N-terminal regulatory domain (RD) and stabilizes half-zippered complexes dependent on RD. Experiments showed Munc8 prevents SNAP25 dissociation during t-SNARE unfolding, enabling de novo assembly: 5% of unfolded SNARE complexes reassembled with 1 µM Munc8. Three Munc8 activities align with prior binding modes, notably enhancing de novo assembly by holding SNAP2 with syntaxin/VAMP. The model proposes Munc governs all SNARE stages, chaperoning intermediates to ensure proper zippering and fusion. Mutations affecting SNARE-SM interactions support fusion, indicating Munc’s focus on intermediates. Findings clarify SNARE domain functions and Munc’s chaperone role in exyt.
The study reveals that integrin adhesomes are pre-assembled in the cytosol as multi-protein building blocks, enabling rapid and modular self-assembly of cell-matrix adhesion sites. Using FCCS and FRAP, researchers found that stationary focal adhes symmetrically exchange the same protein complexes with the cytos, maintaining spatiotemporal uniformity. Cytosolic blocks remain size-confined due to interaction dependencies, forming functional layers for anchoring, force transduction, and actin regulation. Symmetric exchange preserves block identity, preventing aberrant structures. This mechanism ensures modular, controlled self-assembly while retaining system logic.
This study identifies a non-oscillatory electrophysiological marker, the 1/f slope of the power spectrum (0–5 Hz), that distinguishes wakefulness from states of reduced arousal, including propof anesthesia, non-rapid eye movement (NREM) sleep, and rapid eye movement (REM) sleep, using intracranial and scalp EEG across four datasets. The slope becomes steeper (more negative) in propof, NREM, and REM compared to wakefulness, reliably tracking arousal levels across sub-second to full-night timescales. Critically, it differentiates wake from REM despite REM’s desynchronized EEG, aligning with cortical excitability changes in slow-wave up/down states, linking spectral dynamics to population synchrony. Findings show the1 slope correlates with arousal across sleep stages and anesthesia, offering a unified metric for neurophysiological state assessment.
Stromules, tubular extensions from chloroplasts, guide perinuclear chloroplast movement during innate immunity via microtubule (MT)-mediated extension and actin filament (AF)-mediated anchoring. Stromules extend along MTs, with MT stabilization increasing their frequency and length, while AFs anchor but do not drive extension. Stromule direction correlates with chloroplast movement, observed even without immune induction, suggesting a general role in epidermal cells. During immunity, MTs and AFs facilitate perin chloroplast clustering. A model proposes stromules extend along MTs toward nuclei, attaching to nuclear AF anchors, directing chloroplasts to nuclei. MT depolymerization retracts stromules; stabilization enhances them. Cytoskeletal interactions reveal MT-driven strom extension and AF anchoring as complementary, enabling stromule-directed organelle movement and perin clustering. Future work will explore molecular mechanisms and immune relevance of perin chloroplast positioning.
The study demonstrates that evolutionary covariance between amino acid residues at protein–protein interfaces can robustly predict physical contacts, enabling structure modeling of complexes. Using a pseudo-likelihood method, researchers identified covarying pairs in 5S ribosomal units and8 bacterial complexes with known structures, showing high accuracy when sequence alignments exceed protein lengths. Applied to6 complexes without structures, they predicted contacts for TRAP transporter, efflux systems, pyruvate lyase-activating enzyme, and methionine ABC transporter. Covarying pairs in 3D structures were frequent for interacting proteins, rare for non-interacting. Method outperforms prior coevolution-based approaches beyond histidine-kinase systems. Sufficient sequence data is critical for reliability. Results validate evolutionary covariance as a tool for interface prediction and interaction detection.
The study investigates how humans use active sensing strategies to categorize visual patterns by integrating information from multiple scene locations. Using a gaze-conting paradigm, participants revealed image regions sequentially through fixations, with their eye movements guided by prior knowledge and scene statistics. Categorization accuracy improved when fixations followed an optimal Bayesian active algorithm, revealing that suboptimal performance stemmed from prior biases, perceptual noise, saccadic inaccuracies, and inefficient selection (7% efficiency). Fixation-by-fixation analysis dissociated eye-movement inefficiencies from perceptual and decision processes, quantifying suboptimality sources. Participants outperformed heuristic strategies but lagged behind optimal models incorporating their own biases. Controlled patterns and foveal apertures enabled precise information quantification, showing eye movements aimed to maximize category-relevant information requiring multi-location integration. This framework extends active sensing to non-spatial tasks, enabling principled efficiency assessment across perceptual domains.

A two-parameter model links import flux to energetic requirements, revealing multivalent LS binding and size as key determinants. Previous studies focused on small cargoes; this extends to large entities like pre-ribosomes. Model assumes no inter-cargo interactions during transport. Validated via fluorescent controls and dextran. Findings clarify principles for nuclear transport, aiding understanding of export mechanisms.
