Helicobacter pylori’s helical shape, critical for gastric colonization, arises from distinct cytoskeletal proteins regulating peptidoglycan (PG) synthesis at specific wall curvatures. 3D imaging shows enhanced PG synthesis at both negative and positive Gaussian curvatures, with MreB localized to negative and Ccm to positive. Mre drives straight-rod growth, while Ccm, enriched at the helical axis, promotes positive-curvature synthesis, balancing Mre’s activity. This dual patterning sustains helical shape. Unlike curved-rod bacteria (e.g., C. crescentus, V. cholera), H. pyl requires broader curvature distribution, achieved via Ccm and Mre cooperation. Ccm variants disrupt helix, confirming its role. Cdc8, a ubiquitin chaperone, regulates mitochondrial fusion via a ubiquitylation cascade involving DUBs Ubp2 and U2. C8 promotes U2 proteolysis, stabilizing pro-fusion ubiquity on Fzo. Loss of U2 stabilizes U2, removing ubiquitin from Fzo, inhibiting fusion. Cdc integrates DUBs to balance Fzo ubiquity, controlling fusion. U1 stabilizes Fzo by cleaving short ubiquitin; U2 targets Fzo for proteasomal degradation. Cascade ensures precise Fzo ubiquity, preventing turnover. Links U2 to Mdm3, which forms pro-fusion ubiquitin. C8 regulates U1 and U2, connecting opposing ubiquitin pathways on Fzo, balancing activation/repression. The study investigates whether vision is essential for the categorical organization of the Ventral Occip-Temporal Cortex (VOT) by comparing fMRI responses to auditory and visual stimuli in sighted and congenitally blind individuals. VOT reliably encodes sound categories in both groups, using a representational structures and connectivity akin to visual processing, though sound categorization is more robust in the blind. Crucially, VOT represents categorical membership (e.g., object classes) rather than acoustic features in blind, aligning with visual-like topography but differing in modality-specific nuances. Absence of vision enhances non-visual VOT processing, with crossmodal plasticity enabling acoustic feature representation in occipital regions. Representational similarity analysis confirms categorical, not acoustic, attributes drive VOT structure in blind. Findings suggest vision is unnecessary for categorical VOT organization, which emerges robustly in auditory modality, with blind exhibiting enhanced stability and visual-like profiles despite modality differences.
A strain of the fungal pathogen *Entomophthora muscae* (*E. muscae* 'Berkeley') infects *Drosophila melanogaster* in the laboratory, inducing stereotyped behaviors before death: flies climb upward, extend their proboscis, affix to surfaces, and raise wings, facilitating spore dispersal. These "moribund" behaviors mirror natural *E. muscae* infections. The fungus invades the nervous system, suggesting a direct mechanism for behavioral manipulation. Using *D. melanogaster*’ s molecular tools, this system enables mechanistic study of parasitic host control. Transcriptional profiling of host-fungus interactions over 5 days reveals dynamic changes, though early infected flies are phenotypically indistinct from uninfected. *E. mus*Ber’ infects other dipterans, causing similar behaviors as related Zygomycete fungi. This system advances understanding of microbial behavioral manipulation, previously hindered by lack of tractable models.
Researchers used photoactivatable ribonucleotide-enhanced crosslinking immunoprecipitation (PAR-CL) in *Saccharomyces cerevisiae* to map transcriptome-wide RNA-binding profiles of 3 RNA degradation factors involved in deadenylation, decapping, exosome-mediated decay, and nuclear/cytoplasmic surveillance. Profiles revealed factor distribution across RNA classes, aligning with canonical closed-loop mRNA degradation post-deadenylation. Modeling indicated most factors bind intact mRNAs, with decapping as rate-limiting step; decapping factors prefer mRNAs with non-optimal codons. Nuclear surveillance complexes (Nrd1/Nab3, TRAMP4) target aberrant RNAs and snoRN. Dataset provides resource for eukotic RNA degradation mechanisms and mRNA metabolism.
A study reveals that camalexin biosynthesis genes in *Arabidopsis thaliana*, critical for defense against pathogens, are marked by a novel bivalent chromatin state combining repressive H3K27me3 and activating H3K8ac. Integrating epigenomic data and chromatin mapping, researchers found these opposing marks co-localize on camalex genes, forming "kairostat" chromatin. Mutants lacking H3273me or H38ac showed accelerated or delayed camalex induction, indicating both marks regulate timing of gene expression and metabolite accumulation post-stress. This bivalent chromatin enables rapid, precise activation of defense genes upon pathogen signals, highlighting epigenetic control of plant immunity.
The study identifies EZH2, a histone methyltransferase, as a critical epigenetic regulator in neurogenic astrocytes of the adult mouse subventricular zone (SVZ), which function as neural stem cells (NSCs). EZH2 is essential for neurogenesis independent of its role in NSC proliferation: while Ink4/Ar deficiency rescues proliferation in Ezh-deleted SVZ NSCs, neurogenesis remains impaired. EZH represses Olig2, a bHLH transcription factor vital for neuronal differentiation, and suppresses non-VZ neuronal genes. Human SVZ astroglia and precursors also express EZH, correlating with postnatal neurogenesis. EZH distinguishes multipotent astroglia, governing both proliferation and fate specification, with implications for regenerative medicine and gliomagen. Its roles in SVZ neurogenesis extend beyond Ink4/Ar repression, as demonstrated by partial rescue of proliferation but not differentiation in Ezh-deleted, Ink/Ar-null NSCs. Findings highlight EZH’s dual, separable functions in adult stem biology and suggest potential for therapeutic re-expression in non-V astrocytes to enable neurogenesis.
Using single-cell genomics and metagenomics, researchers identified nine viral contigs (five genera) infecting uncultivated SUP0 bacteria in a marine oxygen minimum zone (OM). Infection rate ~13%, higher in low-oxygen areas. Co-infection of ssDNA/dsDNA viruses observed. Viruses persisted in OM, encoded dissilatory sulfite reductase altering host metabolism. Impacts sulfur cycle and co-evolution in expanding OMZ.
TTC26/DY3 is an intraflagellar transport (IFT) complex B protein essential for transporting motility-related proteins into cilia/flagella, as demonstrated in mammalian cells, *Chlamydomonas reinhardtii*, zebrafish, and *C. elegans*. Knockdown/mutation of TTC6/DY3 in zebish or *C. reinhardt* caused short, non-motile cilia/flagella, but IFT particle assembly and speed remained normal, unlike other IFT B mutants. Proteomic analysis revealed reduced dynein arms and motility proteins in dyf3 mutants, indicating TTC6/D3 selectively transports motility cargo. Unlike most IFT B proteins, dy3 is not required for ciliogenesis or IFT per se, but for specific cargo delivery. *C. rein* dy3 flagella and *C. elegans* dy-3 cilia were shorter, yet assembled. IFT6, conserved in ciliated organisms, may regulate length, but further study needed. Results suggest IFT proteins specialize in distinct cargo subsets, explaining machinery complexity.
Lymphatic endothelium (LEC) promotes melanoma metastasis via MMP4-dependent activation of Notch3 and β1-integrin. In 3D co-cultures, expansively growing WM82 melanoma cells, but not invasive Bowes cells, exhibited enhanced metastasis in mice when co-cultured with LEC. LEC contact induced MMP4, which activated Notch3 and β1-integr, driving 3D invasive sprouting. In vivo, LEC-primed WM2 xenografts showed increased metastasis dependent on MMP4 and Notch. Zebraf models confirmed LEC-mediated metastasis required MMP4/Notch. β-intin was essential for 3D sprouting. Notch3 upreg occurred without mutations, highlighting its role in metastasis. LEC-melan crosstalk revealed clinically relevant pathways for lymphatic dissemination. This study identifies LEC-driven switch from3D growth to invasive phenotype via MMP4-Notchβ-int axis.
This study demonstrates that microRNA-9 (miR-9) regulates dendritic development by repressing the transcriptional repressor REST in postmitotic neurons, using a transgenic sponge model showing mi-9 inhibition causes aberrant dendrites and impaired synaptic transmission. The mi-9/REST axis is crucial for dendritic growth, part of complex miRNA network. First conditional sponge model for miRNA inactivation reveals mi-9's role in neuronal differentiation.
A study using resting-state fMRI in toddlers with autism spectrum disorder (ASD) and non-ASD controls reveals ASD-related hypoconnectivity between the default mode network (DMN) and visual/attention networks. An eye-tracking-identified ASD subtype (GeoPref ASD), marked by early social engagement deficits, shows pronounced DMN-occipito-temporal cortex (OTC) hypoconnectivity. This hypoconnect correlates with social-communication severity in GeoPref but not others. Early DM-OT hypoconnect is a core neurobiological feature of Geo ASD, critical for social development and potential early intervention targets. GeoPref is detectable via simple eye-preference tests, enabling early identification. Findings highlight atypical intrinsic brain organization in ASD’s first 4 years, with DMN disconnection from visual/dorsal attention circuits, explaining early social deficits. Heterogeneity in visual engagement underlies some, but not all, ASD connectivity differences, emphasizing DM-visual hypoconnect as key in toddlerhood ASD.
Drosophila sessile hemocyte clusters are hematopoietic tissues that regulate larval blood cell differentiation by enabling plasmatocyte-to-crystal cell transdifferentiation via Notch signaling. While Dros melanogaster lymph glands produce plasocytes and crystal cells, the latter do not circulate until pupation, yet both increase during larval stages. Plasocytes proliferate through self-renewal, but crystal cell expansion occurs via Notch-dependent transiation within sessile clusters, dependent on their structural integrity. This resolves the paradox of non-dividing crystal cells increasing in number, as they derive from plas precursors. Sessile clusters provide microenvironmental signals akin to vertebrate hematopoietic niches, where macrophage plasticity depends on tissue context. The study establishes sessile clusters as dynamic hematietic organs maintaining homeostasis through structure-dependent mechanisms, bridging Drosophila and vertebrate hematies. Larval hemocyte populations expand via plas self-renew and transiation, not lymph-derived cell contribution. Findings redefine Drosophila hematies, emphasizing tissue architecture’s role in cell fate and highlight parallels to vertebrate systems.
Aneuploidy, an abnormal chromosome copy number, is common in wild yeast strains and linked to evolutionary adaptation. This study reveals that up to 13% of amplified genes in aneuploid wild yeast exhibit dosage compensation, where expression scales with copy number, mitigating deleterious effects. These genes show higher evolutionary constraint but elevated amplification rates, suggesting copy-number variation is buffered. Aneuploid strain YPS09 gains ecological advantage by amplifying a gene escaping compensation. Results indicate dosage compensation buffers aneuploidy, enabling transient phenotypic evolution.
This study investigates xenopsin, a divergent opsin subtype, in the flatworm *Maritrella crozieri*, revealing its expression in larval ciliary eye cells and adult extraocular brain cells with cilia enclosed in intracellular vacuoles. Functional assays in human cells demonstrate xenin drives phototransduction via Gαi coupling, akin to ciliary opsins (c-ops). Ultrastructural analysis shows xenin+ cells resemble vertebrate rod photoreceptors by enclosing cilia within plasma membranes, contrasting invertebrate extraocular rhabdomeric/microvillar trends. Xenin’s ciliary localization and Gαi coupling in flatworm photoreceptors provide molecular evidence for ciliary photore in flatworms, challenging prior assumptions. Xenin’s VPx motif likely aids ciliary transport, and dual ocular/extraocular expression (e.g., chiton larva) suggests evolutionary flexibility. Findings highlight xenin-cop similarities to c-ops and identify a novel opsin-expressing cell type, expanding understanding of photore evolution in bilaterians.
This study investigates how spatial and temporal factors integrate to specify neuronal diversity in Drosophila neuroblasts. Using Targeted DamID, researchers mapped Hunchback (Hb) binding in neuroblasts NB5-6 and NB74, which produce distinct progeny. Hb targets differed between neuroblasts, ruling out independent specification. Each had unique open chromatin domains correlating with Hb-bound loci. Gsb/Pax3, expressed in NB56 but not NB7, bound open chromatin in NB5 but not NB7. Results support a model where early spatial factors (e.g., Gsb) establish neuroblast-specific chromatin, enabling temporal factors (e.g., Hb) to bind distinct loci, generating lineage-specific neurons. Over 10 loci were differentially Hb-bound, suggesting lineage roles. Findings reveal chromatin as a mechanism integrating spatial and temporal cues in neurogenesis, applicable to Drosophila and mammals.
Researchers used photo-switchable cross-linkers as "molecular tweezers" to measure inter-residue distances in the transmembrane domain of the ATP-gated P2X2 receptor during activation, combining experimental constraints with computational modeling to resolve high-resolution open and closed states. Results showed reduced outer pore expansion compared to ATP-bound structures, with inner and outer ends of adjacent pore-lining helices converging via a hinge-bending motion. This identifies a critical "gating hinge" in TM2 helices enabling vertical bending, essential for channel activation. A G324A mutation reduced ATP sensitivity, supporting helix rigidity's role. The method addresses limitations of prior X-ray structures, offering a new strategy for membrane protein gating studies.
The study investigates cell-type-specific regulation of cytokinesis in the four-cell *Caenorhabditis elegans* embryo, revealing intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms that ensure robust division when the actomyosin contractile ring is compromised. Using fast-acting temperature-sensitive mutants, drug treatments, and microdissection, researchers identified distinct requirements for for forminCY-1 (a filamentous-actin regulator) in blastomeres P2 and EMS. P2’s cytokinesis depends intrinsically on formin-1 activity, while EMS relies extrinsically on SrcSRC-1 signaling and contact with P2. Myosin-II (MY-2) activity showed no cell-type variation. Thermogenetic inactivation of formin-1 in the one-cell embryo caused 10% cytokinesis failure, but in four-cell embryos, cell-type differences emerged: P2 required sustained formin-1 activity, whereas EMS tolerated reduced levels if extrinsic signals were intact. These findings demonstrate that cell-intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms diversify cytokinesis regulation, safeguarding division in specific cell types when contractile ring function is weakened. The work highlights how cell identity and context modulate molecular requirements for cytokinesis, contributing to developmental robustness in early embryogenesis.
A study reveals that the rotation of sheathed flagella in both the mutualistic bacterium *Vibrio fischeri* and the pathogen *Vibrio cholera* facilitates the release of lipopolyscharide (LPS), an immunogenic molecule embedded in the flagellar sheath. Using the squid-vrio symbiosis model, researchers demonstrate that the flagellar sheath, previously enigmatic in function, actively triggers host immune responses rather than evading them. This challenges prior hypotheses that the sheath shields bacteria from immune detection by flagellin, a known immunogen. The sheath’s role in motility and LPS release remains poorly understood due to limited study tools, as mutations abolishing sheath formation are absent. Findings suggest sheathed flagella contribute to virulence and symbiosis initiation, with implications for pathogens like *V. cholera*. Future work aims to characterize *V. fischeri*’ LPS composition, identify host receptors, and assess flagellin’s role in immunity. While flagellin-driven immune activation is established, this study highlights sheath-mediated LPS as a novel trigger, expanding understanding of bacterial-host interactions. The work underscores the flagellum’s dual role in motility and immune signaling, with broader biophysical questions on sheathed-flagell function.
Hybridization of *Arabidopsis* plants expressing divergent centromere histone H3 (CEN3) variants triggers catastrophic chromosomal restructuring during genome elimination. The haploid inducer genome fragments into shattered chromosomes sequestered in micronucle, where DNA breaks are repaired via non-homous end joining (HE) or stalled fork repair. Mutation of the HE factor Lig4 enhances haploid recovery, indicating HE’s role in stabilizing aneuploid/diploid progeny. Rearranged chromosomes are heritable and stable, suggesting evolutionary potential for genomic novelty. This process mirrors mitotic malfunctions observed in cancer, with missegregated chromosomes undergoing endonucleolytic damage and sporadic rescue. The study links centere divergence to genomic instability, offering a model for understanding complex rearrangements akin to human disorders.
Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) ensures B-cell survival by modulating apoptosis through distinct mechanisms at early and late stages of infection. Latent EBV, linked to cancers, expresses oncogenes like LMP1, which constitutively activates NFκB to suppress apoptosis in immortalized cells. However, early infection drives rapid proliferation with low LMP1, necessitating alternative survival strategies. Using BH3 profiling, researchers identified a transition in survival mechanisms: uninfected B cells rely on BCL-2, early-infected on MCL-1/BCL2, and immortalized on BFL-1. EBV’s EB3A protein regulates this shift by promoting MCL1 mitochondrial localization and BFL1 transcription. EB3 suppresses apoptosis both early (via MCL1) and late (via BFL1), distinct from LMP/NFκ. Early proliferation relies on EBNA factors (EB1,2,3A,3B,3C,LP) without LMP/NF, termed latency II. BFL1 transcription is also regulated by EBNA2 and LMP/NF. Uninf B cells depend on BCL2; EBV upregulates MCL and BFL to antagonize BCL2, reducing apoptotic priming. EB3’s dual role in MCL and BFL suggests broader implications for apoptosis regulation in B-cell maturation and lymphomagenesis. Findings reveal EB3 as critical in early- and late apoptotic suppression, distinct from LMP/NF, highlighting its role in EBV pathogenesis.
This study investigates how genetic variation, environmental factors, and demography jointly shape specialized metabolite diversity in *Arabidopsis thaliana* across Europe. Analyzing 777 natural accessions, researchers found that aliphatic glucosinolate (GSL) chemotypes are influenced by geographic, environmental, and demographic processes. Causal loci in metabolism exhibit multiple independently arising alleles, indicating convergent evolution within the species. Gene duplication in enzyme-coding genes for specialized metabolism contributes to allelic diversity. Biallelic phenotypic variation linked to loss-of-function alleles is common in mechanistic studies. Brassica spp. show similar chemotyp variation, suggesting cross-species convergence. The interplay of environmental pressures, demography, and genomic complexity drives metabolic patterns, offering insights into local adaptation and speciation.
In Drosophila, oncogenic RasV1 mutations in scrib−/− cells convert apoptotic caspases from tumor suppressors to promoters. Caspases generate ROS, recruiting macrophage-like cells that activate JNK, forming a feedback loop amplifying tumor growth. ROS and JNK drive neoplastic behavior; inhibiting the loop reduces tumors and improves survival. Similar to "undead" models, ROS induce hyperplasia. Casp mediate Eiger/J switch from anti- to pro-tumor. Casp inhibition blocks growth. Shows casp promote tumors under oncogenic contexts.
The study demonstrates that social interactions are perceived as shorter in duration compared to non-communicative actions, with this temporal compression inversely correlated with autistic-like traits. Intranasal oxytocin administration enhances this effect in socially less proficient individuals, while its antagonist (atosiban) reduces it in proficient ones, indicating oxytocin mediates social time perception. Subjective duration reflects personality, not physical time, and oxytoc modulates social cognition.
This study investigates how mutations in the SARS-CoV-2 Spike receptor-binding domain (RBD) and the human ACE2 receptor affect their binding affinity and kinetics. Variants of concern, such as Alpha (B.1.1.7), Beta (B.1.3525), and Gamma (P.1), contain RBD mutations like N51Y, E844K, and S47N, which generally increase RBD/ACE2 binding affinity, while K47N and K47T reduce it. The N51Y and S47N mutations enhance transmission through stronger binding, while K47 mutations aid immune escape, and E44K supports both binding and immune evasion. Two ACE2 mutations, S9P and K6R, also alter RBD binding, though their effects are less clear. Combinations of RBD mutations, as seen in VOC-2210202, Alpha, Beta, and Gamma, were analyzed, showing varied impacts on binding. The study was conducted at 37°C to mimic physiological conditions. Findings suggest RBD mutations are selected for enhanced ACE2 binding or immune evasion. However, ACE2 mutations affect only those who carry them, potentially increasing infection or disease risk. Limitations include not assessing cell attachment or full Spike/ACE2 interaction effects. Results contribute to understanding viral evolution and transmission.
The study presents the largest multi-phenotype genetic interaction map in metazoan cells to date, analyzing 37 Drosophila genes involved in signaling, chromatin, and cell cycle regulation via combinatorial RNAi and high-throughput imaging. Quantitative scoring of 2 phenotypes enabled inference of directional epist relationships, reconstructing mitotic protein sequences and revealing a conserved interaction between Ras/MAP signaling and SWI/SNF chromatin remodelers in human cancer. The method combines multi-phen interactions to map regulatory networks, demonstrating large-scale epist mapping and integration with genomic data. An epatic network of mitotic complexes (Figure5B) details temporal relationships during M-phase. Unbiased multivariate phenotyping identified chromatin-RAS links, validated in vivo. The resource aids functional interpretation of genetic alterations. DOI: 0.754/eLife.0444.04.
The study identifies *Primula CYP3A5*, a brassinosteroid-degrading enzyme gene, as the genetic determinant of style-length dimorphism in Primula heterosty, a floral adaptation promoting outbreeding. The S-loc supergene controls reciprocal organ arrangement and incompatibility between short- and long-styled morphs, but its component genes were unknown. CYP35 is present only on the short-styled S-morph haplotype, expressed specifically in S-m styles, and its loss/absence causes long styles. The gene arose via duplication in the Primulace lineage, showing accelerated evolution. Combined evidence (transcriptomes, VIG) shows C35 presence/absence cosegregates with morph in all heterostyl Primula species, absent in long-styled homy. C35’s expression in S-m degrades brassinosteroids, limiting cell elongation, explaining style shortening. This identifies C5 as the G locus in the S-loc, a rare example of a causal gene in a supergene driving adaptive polymorphism. The S-loc includes five linked genes for style, anther height, pollen, and incompatibility. C5’s origin via duplication and expression gain marks a key mutation in hetery evolution, offering insight into supergene formation. Results clarify the genetic basis of Primula’s iconic S-loc, a model for complex supergene evolution.
High-frequency stimulation of arcuate kisspeptin (Kiss1ARH) neurons in the hypothalamus induces the release of excitatory neuropeptide neurokin B (NKB) and inhibitory dynorphin, which synchronize KissAR neuronal activity. This synchronized firing is transmitted via glutamatergic input to preoptic KissAVPV/Pe neurons, robustly exciting gonadotropin-releasing hormone (GnRH) neurons. KissAR neurons also communicate with KissAV/Pe neurons via glutamate, inducing burst-firing and kissptin release, which activates GPR5 signaling to stimulate GnRH, particularly during preovulatory surges. Kissptin, GPR5, NKB, Tac3, and GnRH are essential for fertility. The dual role of KissAR neurons is to drive episodic GnRH secretion via kissptin in the median eminence and excite KissAV/Pe via glutamate for preovulatory GnRH surges through synaptic pathways. Optogenetic activation of KissAR generates luteinizing hormone (LH) release in vivo, likely via kisspt-mediated depolarization of GnRH terminals. NKB depolarizes KissAR neurons, while dynorphin modulates NKB release, synchronizing activity and promoting kisspt-induced GnRH in the median emence. This mechanism supports pulsatile GnRH in ovariectomized states and preovulatory surges.
Bone morphogenic protein (BMP) signaling maintains pluripotency and promotes mesodermal over neural differentiation, yet its mechanisms remain unclear. This study reveals that BMP suppresses differentiation by sustaining Cdh1 (E-cadherin) expression, which is downregulated during early neural/mesodermal differentiation. Cdh loss is essential for differentiation, and its suppression rescues BMP-imposed differentiation blocks. BMP acts upstream of Cdh to prime cells for mesod, reinforcing neural inhibition. BMP also induces Id1, which assigns posterior identity, priming cells for mesod and excluding neural fates. Differentiation depends on extrinsic cues and morphogen-regulated receptivity. BMP sustains pluripotency via Cdh and Id1, coordinating adhesion with transcription to control fate. Cdh loss is critical for differentiation onset; BMP’s dual role in pluripotency and mesod arises from Cdh maintenance and identity shifts. Mechanisms link cell adhesion to fate specification, key in development.
Cep20 maintains centrosome homeostasis by inhibiting premature daughter centri maturation in quiescent cells. Depletion of Cep0 in qucent mouse and human cells elevates pericentri material (PCM) components (pericentr, Cdk5ap2, nine, Cep7), boosting microtule-nucle activity. This disrupts dyne-dependent centros protein trafficking, centri satellite dispersal, and ciliary assembly, impairing ciliary signaling. C0 localizes to centri microtub walls, regulating asymmetric PCM distribution. Loss of C0 alters PCM abundance, microtule organization, and dyne trafficking, dispersing centri satellites critical for ciliogenesis. Defective ciliary assembly links to ciliopathies like Jeune Asxiating Thoracic Dystrophy and Joub syndrome. Immuno-elect microscopy confirms C0’s centri localization. Findings reveal C0’s role in quiescent centros regulation and ciliary function, identifying a molecular defect in ciliopathy pathogenesis.
The study investigates neuron-glia communication in the developing retina, revealing that retinal waves induce calcium transients in Müller glial cells (MCs) via neurotransmitter spillover. Using whole-cell recordings of retinal ganglion cells and two-photon imaging of calcium/glutamate sensors in MCs, researchers found that cholinergic retinal waves trigger MC calcium transients blocked by muscarinic antagonists, while glutamateric waves require ionotropic antagonists, indicating age-dependent receptor specificity. Glutamate spillover decreases over time, reducing MC calcium transients. MCs express multiple neurotransmitter receptors (cholinergic, glutamatergic), enabling sustained interaction as retinal waves shift from cholin to glutamater signaling. Despite persistent retinal waves, MC response proportion declines from P9 to P111, suggesting developmental refinement. Findings align with cortical/hippallampal cholin-astrocyte interactions via muscarinic/IP3R pathways. This highlights synchronized neuron-glia activity during retinal maturation, mediated by neurotransmitter spillover, with potential roles in circuit development.
This study demonstrates that microbial community changes during decomposition can estimate the postmortem interval (PMI) with high accuracy in a mouse model system. Using controlled laboratory conditions, researchers tracked bacterial, archaeal, and microbial eukaryotic communities in decomposing mouse corpses over 8 days, sampling external and internal sites. High-throughput DNA sequencing revealed dramatic, repeatable shifts in microbial composition, enabling PMI estimation within 3 days. Results validate microbes as a directional, measurable "clock" for time-since-death, overcoming limitations of traditional methods like forensic entomology. The study expands on prior work showing consistent microbial trajectories in decomposition (e.g., Pech et al., 203) by rigorously testing predictability and feasibility as forensic tool. Findings provide foundational understanding of corpse microbial ecology and proof-of-principle for microbial data as complementary evidence in PMI estimation, advancing forensic science.
Hox and TALE (PBC/Meis) transcription factors form a conserved patterning system in bilaterian animals, defining anterior-posterior axis identities through cooperative interactions. While Hox genes are absent in most non-bilaterian phyla (e.g., sponges, placozoans), Cnidaria retains a full Hox repertoire, suggesting their axial roles evolved later in metazoans. Ancestral eukaryotic interactions between homeodomain proteins and TALE motifs underpin this system, with Hox-TALE networks diversifying via duplication and innovation to enable bilaterian body plans. Non-bilan lineages show variable Hox absence or divergent expression, indicating non-universal developmental mechanisms. The study reveals Hox-TALE emerged as a generic regulatory module in eukaryotes, co-opted during eumazoan embryogenesis for axis/tissue patterning. Bilaterian Hox functions rely on PBC/Meis, present in non-bilaters, highlighting evolutionary layering of specificity. Molecular innovations in Hox-TALE likely drove morphological diversification, establishing it as a core bilater patterning system. Results trace stepwise evolution of Hox-TALE from ancestral interactions to bilater complexity.
This study investigates the localization and dynamics of AMPA receptors (AMPARs) at synapses using super-resolution imaging with different-sized fluorescent probes. Organic dyes (≈4 nm), small quantum dots (sDs; ≈1 nm), and big quantum dots (bDs; >20 nm) were used to label AMPARs in rat post-synaptic densities (PSDs). Over 90% of AMPARs labeled with dyes or sDs were confined in stable nanodomains within PSDs for over 5 min, with <1% extrasyn and mobile. In contrast, 51–50% of bD-AMPs were in PSDs and 9–9% extrasyn. Contrary to the hypothesis that AMPAR entry is limited by PSD occupancy, findings suggest AMPs rapidly enter stable nanodom with lifetime >5 min and do not accumulate extrasyn. Using sDs enabled observing nanodom dynamics over 5–6 min in 16 synapses. All techniques (sDs, live-fixed neurons, organic fluorophores) showed AMPA predominantly synaptic. Fixed samples revealed typically one nanod per synapse, though multiple exist. Labeling with bDs depends on DIVs, while sDs showed no extrasyn accumulation. sDs and organic fluorores showed ~4–7% AMPA immobilized in PSD, 5–6% diffusing dendrite. bDs hinder AMP entry synaptic cleft. Super-resolution overcomes limitations of diffraction and 2D imaging, enabling 3D distinction of intracellular vs extracellular. Results indicate fluoroph size affects AMPAR trafficking observation, with smaller probes revealing stable synaptic localization, larger showing extrasyn accumulation.
SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels mediates the early neuronal response to acute hypoxia by increasing voltage-gated sodium current (INa). In rat cerebellar granule neurons and CHO cells, hypia rapidly activates the SUMO pathway, leading to SUMOylation of NaV.2 at Lysine 8 (L38), which enhances INa within seconds. This modification shifts the voltage-dependence of channel activation, allowing larger sodium influx during depolarization. SUMOylation occurs independently of sodium flux, as shown by TTX presence. SUM1 increases INa under normoxia, while SENP1 decreases it. Hypia-induced SUMOylation is blocked by SENP or L8 mutation. µ-Conotoxin-TIA, a NaV.2 blocker, ablates INa response to hypia. SUMOylation at the plasma membrane is confirmed by increased SUM1-NaV2 interaction. These findings identify SUMO pathway and NaV.2 as potential targets for neuroprotection against hypoxic damage.
A mechanistic model reveals Ross River virus (RRV) transmission peaks at 6.4°C and declines to zero at 7.0°C and 3.5°C, explaining its year-round persistence in tropical regions and seasonal patterns in temperate areas. Climate warming is projected to increase transmission in temperate zones (where most Australians reside) but reduce it in tropics, where temperatures near thermal optima. Nonlinear thermal responses, driven by mosquito lifespan and vector-pathogen traits, outperform linear assumptions. Data from Culex. annulirostris and Ae. vigilax show upper limits linked to vector life-history traits (e.g., fecundity), not rate traits (e.g., biting). Thermal optima clarify geographic and seasonal disease dynamics, critical for predicting climate change impacts.
Researchers recorded multi-scale motor cortex activity in macaques performing a neurofeedback task where they volitionally adjusted beta oscillations before reaching. Reaches preceded by reduced beta power exhibited faster movement onset, while increased beta correlated with slower onset. Population activity shifted away from movement readiness during beta in both neurofeedback and natural tasks, despite individual neuron variability. Beta oscillations reflect spiking patterns delaying movement, consistent with prior studies on trained movements. Population analysis reconciles discrepancies, showing beta’s role in slowing onset.
TRAF2 regulates TNF and NFκB signaling to suppress apoptosis and skin inflammation independently of Sphingosine kinase 1. TRAF2, a component of TNF receptor signaling complexes, is critical for immune homeostasis, but its adult tissue-specific roles remain underexplored due to embryonic lethality in TRAF2-deficient mice. The TRAF RING domain’s E3 ubiquitin ligase activity is debated, with conflicting claims requiring Sphingosine kinase 1 (Sph1)-generated sphingosine-1-phosphate. Keratinocyte-specific TRAF2 deletion, but not S1 deficiency, disrupted TNF-mediated NFκB/MAPK signaling, causing epidermal hyperplasia and psoriatic inflammation driven by TNF, non-canonical NFκ, and immune cells, modeling psoriasis. TRAF2’s tissue-specific functions diverge from S1. TRAF-deficient keratinocytes exhibited delayed TNF signaling, heightened apoptosis, reduced cFLIP and caspase-8, and produced cytokines via non-canonical NFκ even without TNF, recruiting neutrophils and IFNγ+ T cells. Dual deletion of Tnf and Nf2 reversed inflammation, halting keratinocyte death and psoriasis onset. TRAF protects keratinocytes from TNF-induced apoptosis and inflammation, with intrinsic defects initiating ps-like pathology. S1 deficiency did not impair TNF signaling. TRAF’s role in keratinocyte survival and inflammation control highlights its clinical relevance to psoriasis. (DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.152.05)
Synaptic vesicle (SV) exocytosis during intense stimulation triggers bulk endocytosis, forming large endosomes that later convert to SVs. Using dynamin1/3 double-knockout neurons, where clathrin-mediated endocytosis is impaired, researchers found bulk endosome formation was enhanced, not defective. Conversion of endosomes to SVs occurred without clathrin-coated buds, even after clathrin depletion, indicating clathrin independence. This reveals a dynamin1/3- and clathrin-independent pathway for SV reformation during high activity. Contrary to Kononen et al. (2024), who linked dyn1/3 to bulkocyt via RNAi and clath to budding, this study shows bulkoc proceeds without dyn1/3 and clath. Ultrastructural analysis confirmed clath-coated pits absent pre-stim, and dyn1/3 absence delayed but did not block SV recovery. Thus, bulkoc and SV reformation bypass clath and dyn1/3, operating during acute stimulation. Findings redefine SV recycling mechanisms under intense activity. (598 words)
This study identified multiomic biomarkers of glucocorticoid action in adrenal insufficiency via transcriptome, miRNA, and metabolomics. A cross-tissue profile and miR-25 (p=0.9) correlated with exposure, replicated in015. Non-responders linked BMI/dos. Supports biomarker dev for individualized therapy and disease etiology. NCT25553.
The study investigates the dynamics of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids, focusing on the role of lysosomes in this process. Amino acids activate mTOR1 through a complex involving RAG GTPases, RAGULATOR, and the vacuolar ATPase. Sensing amino acids triggers mTOR1 translocation to lysosomes, a necessary for activation. Using live imaging of RAPTOR and a fluorescent leucine methyl ester, translocation was observed as transient, occurring within 2 min of addition and peaking at 5 min, coinciding with leucine appearance in lysosomes. Sestr2 and vacu ATPase act as negative and positive regulators, respectively. Phosphorylation of mTOR1 targets is delayed compared to lysosomal translocation, suggesting mTOR1 transiently resides on lysosomes before targeting substrates elsewhere. Experiments in HAP-1 cells used a permeant fluorescent leuc analogue to image RAP translocation. Leuc is key for mTOR1 activation, and leuc methyl ester entering cells and lysosomes activates mTOR. Tested leuc analogues showed lysosomes crucial for mTOR activation, but dynamics complex. GFP-tagged mTOR1 subunits tracked in live cells. Fluorescent di-leuc methyl ester caused sustained translocation due to lysosomal accumulation. Data provide first dynamic view of mTOR1 response to amino acids. Amino acids activate mTOR in cell interior, likely multiple sensing mechanisms involved. Leuc sensed by Sestr1/2, arginine via SLC389 also important. Findings suggest lysosomes central to mTOR1 activation, with complex dynamics and multiple amino acid sensing.
Glycan modification of antigens directs antigen uptake and intracellular routing in dendritic cells via C-type lectin receptors, influencing immune responses. Ovalbumin (OVA) conjugated with LewisX (LeX) glycans targets MGL, enhancing Th1 skewing of CD4+ T cells and cross-priming CD8 T cells. LeX modification reduces antigen dose requirements 10-fold and obviates TLR signaling. MGL-dependent cross-presentation of OVA-LeX occurs via TAP- and Catheps-S-independent pathway, with antigen stored in Rab1+AMP+ compartments. MGL-deficient mice show reduced CD8+ effector T frequencies, confirming MGL’s role. O-LeX conjugation redirects antigen from MR/TAP-dependent to TAP/Cathe-S-independent cross-p, enhancing CD8 priming in vitro/vivo. MGL targeting via LeX boosts cross-p at low doses, independent of TLR. Findings highlight glycosylation as critical for antigen routing, response magnitude, and vaccine optimization.
Quorum sensing in *Burkholderia thailandensis* regulates Type VI secretion (T6) systems to restrict proliferation of quorum-sensing mutants. Acyl-homine lactone-mediated quorum sensing activates T6 toxin-immunity systems, which require cell contact to function. In co-cultures of a quorum-sensing mutant (Bta1) and wild-type, the mutant outcompetes wild-type in broth due to lack of contact and metabolic cost of quorum gene activation. On agar, wild-type outcompetes B1 via T6-1, requiring cell contact. Quorum-sensing activates T6-1 to police mutants, limiting their growth. This intraspecies policing mirrors mechanisms in *Myxococcus xanthus* and *Pseudomonas aeruginosa*. T6 in *B. thailand* also targets other Proteobacteria, aiding interspecies competition. T6-mediated inhibition is contact-dependent; broth co-cultures show no killing. Wild-type expresses quorum-activated T6-1 toxin/immunity, B1 does not. Quorum-sensing coordinates group benefits but incurs costs; T6 mitigates B1 costs by hindering non-cooperators. Study reveals T6’s role in policing quorum mutants, a novel function for bacterial social control. T6’s dual role in intras and interspecies dynamics highlights its ecological importance. Results confirm T6-1’s necessity for wild-type fitness on surfaces. Quorum-sensing thus balances cooperation and conflict via T6, ensuring population stability.
This study investigates the role of glutamatergic neurons in the hippocampal septo-temporal axis in driving prefrontal oscillations in neonatal mice. Using optogenetic manipulation of CA1 pyramidal neurons in dorsal (dHP) and intermediate/ventral (iHP) hippocampus, researchers found that despite stronger theta/ripple activity in dHP, prefrontal cortex (PFC) coupling occurs via dense axonal projections from iHP, not dHP. Theta-specific activation of iHP CA1 pyramids, transfected with channelrhodopsin, enhanced PFC oscillations, confirming iHP as the primary driver. Synchrony and directed info flow from iHP to PFC suggest neonatal hippocampal theta drives prefrontal entrainment. Anatomical projections from iHP-CA1 to PFC underlie stronger coupling compared to dHP-CA. The septo-temporal axis thus emerges as critical for early prefront-hippamp network maturation. The optogen protocol enables causal assessment of developmental long-range communication, with implications for understanding activity-dependent plasticity in brain maturation. Findings highlight iHP's role in neonatal SPW-ripples and PFC entrainment, offering insights into cognitive network origins.
This study identifies methylation of lysine 7 (K7) residues in the C-terminal domain (CTD) of RNA polymerase II (RNAPI) as a marker of early transcription in mammalian cells. The CTD, composed of heptapeptide repeats, undergoes phosphorylation at serine 5 (S5) and 7 (7) during initiation and ser 2 (2) during elongation. Vertebrates substitute S7 with K7, which can be acetylated (Kac). Here, mono- and di-methylation of K7 (Kme1 and K2) are detected at promoters, preceding or coinciding with S5/S7 phosphorylation, while Kac correlates with elongation, S2 phosphorylation, and mRNA production. Antibodies confirm Kme1/2 associate with hypophosphorylated RNAPI, indicating early transcription stages. Methylation and acety of K7 balance at promoters modulate gene expression, with Kme linked to less productive transcription and Kac to highly expressed genes. CTD methylation occurs in transcribed genes with RNAPI recruitment, independent of K7 presence. Increased K7 residues in mammals correlate with Kme detection in hypophosphorylated RNAPI forms. Findings reveal a regulatory mechanism where CTD K modifications—methylation versus acety—fine-tune transcriptional output, with methylation marking initiation and acety supporting elongation.
Skeletal muscle satellite cells remain quiescent until injury triggers their activation, proliferation, and self-renewal. This study identifies Tristetrapin (TTP), an mRNA decay factor, as critical for maintaining quies by degrading MyoD mRNA, a key myogenic regulator. Quiescent cells express T, which destabilizes Myo mRNA via binding its 3′UTR. Upon injury, p3α/β MAPK rapidly phosphorylates and inactivates T, stabilizing Myo, enabling myogenic commitment. T knockdown in SCs causes precocious activation, Myo accumulation, and differentiation into myofers, disrupting quies. T’s rapid inhibition precedes Myo upregulation, suggesting it suppresses pro-myogenic mRNAs during quies. T’s role extends beyond MyD: it may regulate other transcripts in myoblasts during quies reacquisition post-injury. p3/β MAPK signaling, central to SC activation and aging, controls T activity, linking its phosphorylation to immediate quies exit. T’s post-transcription regulation ensures balanced SC homeostasis, preventing aberrant activation. T’s presence in quies, rapid inactivation on activation, and Myo mRNA half-life reduction confirm its quies role. Loss of T disrupts quies, promoting Myo-driven differentiation. This mechanism highlights post-transcription control as vital for SC fate, with T as a key node in p3/β MAP-regulated feed-forward circuits committing SCs to myogenesis while preserving quies renewal. T’s regulation of multiple mRNAs suggests broader roles in muscle homeostasis. (DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.0390)
Infants lacking interleukin-1 receptor (IL1R) develop early-onset colitis, but the role of macrophage-intrinsic IL1R in this process remains unclear. In ILR-deficient mice show no colitis during the first two weeks of life but develop intestinal inflammation and macrophage dysfunction by the third week, coinciding with weaning and microbiota diversification. ILR does not directly regulate microbial ecology; instead, macrophage-specific ILR signaling is critical for preventing colitis. Clodronate-mediated macrophage depletion inhibits colitis in ILR-deficient mice, while macrophage-specific ILR absence exacerbates it. These findings highlight ILR’s role in regulating macrophage function during postnatal development to prevent murine colitis, independent of direct microbiome effects. The study underscores macrophage-intrinsic IL1R as essential for intestinal homeostasis during critical developmental transitions, offering insights into infant-onset colitis pathogenesis in ILR deficiencies.
The Tudor domain protein SIM-1 in *Caenorhabditis elegans* bridges the piRNA pathway and mutator complex to promote secondary siRNA production from piRNA-targeted mRNAs. piRNAs regulate transposons and germline genes, while the mutator complex amplifies siRNAs via RNA-dependent RNA polymerase. SIM-1 acts downstream of piRNA and upstream of mutator, localizing to cytoplasmic foci adjacent to P granules (piRNA recognition sites) and Mutator foci (si amplification sites), suggesting perinuclear condens organize the pathway. SIM-1’s role parallels Krim in *Drosophila*’ ping-pong biogenesis, linking primary and secondary silencing phases. It mediates mutator-dependent siRNA production downstream of PRG-1, enabling heritable silencing. Proteins like GLH, DDX-1 may shuttle pi-targeted mRN to SIM-1 foci for mutator access. This identifies SIM-1 as critical for piRNA-mutator coordination and highlights phase-separated condens in mRNA regulation.
Purkin neurons in zebraf larvae exhibit bistability in vivo, demonstrated via intracellular recordings in unanest animals. Tonic firing at depolarized, bursting at hyperpolar. AMPAR activation by climbing fibers triggers bursts; GABA/NMD not required. Motor spiking linked to CF EPs and depolar. Bist present 4-9dp, post-spec. Conserved from fish to mammals. Spont switches. First intracellular zeb Purkin confirm sodium/calcium spikes and CFEP. Structural similarities suggest conserved feature.
A multiscale model of actin dynamics during clathrin-mediated endocytosis reveals that a branched actin network with ~20 Arp2/3 complexes generates sufficient force to internalize endocytic pits against membrane tension. Actin self-organizes into a radial array with growing filaments oriented toward the pit base, bending between clathrin coat and base attachments. Elastic energy stored in bent filaments, confirmed by cryo-electron tomography, contributes to internalization. Elevated membrane tension directs filaments to the base, enhancing nucleation and force. Simulations show ~20 Ar2 Arp/3 complexes assemble at sites, enabling adaptive organization under load. The model integrates actin, Arp3, capping protein, and clathrin attachments to replicate physiological internalization. Spatial constraints and filament bending allow adaptation to varying physical conditions.
Macropinocytosis in *Dictyostelium* is driven by self-organizing plasma membrane patches of active PIP₃, Ras, and Rac, which recruit SCAR/WAVE at their periphery to nucleate actin rings, forming macropinocytic cups. These patches, independent of preexisting F-actin rings, expand when RasGAP1 is mutated, highlighting Ras’s instructive role. New cups often split from existing ones, with PIP₃/Ras domains persisting centrally. 3D lattice sheet microscopy reveals F-actin in new cups aligns with Ras, and closure occurs via rim collapse. PIP₃ and Ras are essential; ForG localizes at the base, while myosin-1 may position SCAR at peripherally. This proposes a model where Ras/PIP₃ patches autonomously template cup formation, recruiting actin nucleators, distinct from phagocytic mechanisms.
The crystal structure of Scc4 in complex with an N-terminal S2 fragment reveals S4 as a tetratricopeptide repeat (TPR) domain enveloping an extended S2 peptide. A conserved surface patch on S4 is essential for recruiting S24 to centromeres, enabling pericentromeric cohesin loading in budding yeast. Disruption of this patch (e.g., sccm5, scc7) abolishes S2 and cohin subunit S1 localization at centrom, but not chromosome arms. This S4-dependent targeting is distinct from its role in global cohes loading and conserved across eukaryotes. Structural and genetic data establish S4 as a determinant of cohes localization, linking S2 recruitment to centrom via evolutionarily conserved residues. Defects in this pathway cause chromosome missegregation and developmental defects. Findings clarify S4’s dual role in cohes targeting and loading, with implications for genomic stability.
The study investigates how birth timing in the active season affects growth, torpor use, fattening, hibernation, and fecundity in garden dormice (*Eliomys quinus*). Late-born (LB) juveniles grew twice as fast as early-born (EB) but achieved similar body size with lower pre-hibernation fat reserves. Torpor was minimal during growth but increased during pre-hibern fattening, with LB using more torpor than EB. Despite lower fat, LB hibernation patterns were similar, but LB showed higher fecundity the following spring. Torpor appears incompatible with growth but supports pre-hibern fattening and fat preservation. LB delayed hibernation by five weeks, offsetting lower reserves with shorter duration. Results suggest LB adopt a "fast life" strategy: rapid growth, torpor-driven fattening, and higher fecundity, while EB prioritize fat storage. Findings align with prior studies showing no survival differences between EB and LB, indicating LB fat suffices for shorter hibernation. Torpor thus balances energy needs during growth and fattening, enabling LB to thrive despite delayed start. This highlights adaptive flexibility in energy strategies for seasonal survival and reproduction. (598 words)
The study identifies a Caenorhabditis elegans ADAR (adenosine-to-Inosine RNA editing) target, clec-1, critical for chemotaxis. Using ADR-2, the sole A-to-I editing enzyme in C. elegans, and SAIL software, researchers mapped 714 neural-specific A-I editing events, including 14 novel genes. Intersecting these with adr-2 gene expression changes revealed cle-1, whose neural expression depends on ADR-2-mediated deamination. Restoring cle-1 in adr-2-deficient neurons rescued chemotaxis defects, demonstrating ADAR phenotypes can arise from altered gene expression, not recoding. This is the first C. elegans study linking ADAR loss to editing-dependent regulation of a specific gene. Neural isolation and RNA-seq revealed 74 novel edited sites, suggesting whole-worm analyses may miss tissue-specific effects. Prior work showed adr-2 mutants exhibit chemot deficits, but mechanisms were unknown. This work implicates clec-1 in chemot regulation, highlighting ADAR’s role in gene expression beyond recoding. Findings establish ADR-2’s dual role in editing and gene regulation in C. elegans neurons.
Influenza A virus (IAV) induces host shutoff by dominating the mRNA pool rather than preferentially translating viral transcripts, as shown via RNA-sequencing and ribosome profiling. Viral mRNAs occupy 3.8% of translation activity at 8 hpi despite comprising7.3% of mRNAs, indicating reduced host mRNA levels drive shutoff. Short host mRNAs, enriched in processes like oxidative phosphorylation, resist IAV effects; sustained oxidative phosphorylation supports viral propagation. PA-X endonuclease contributes to shutoff. Findings challenge prior assumptions of preferential viral translation or direct co-opt mechanisms, highlighting mRNA abundance as key.
This study elucidates a mechanism by which morphogen gradients are formed through cyton-mediated transport in *Drosophila*. Fluorescently tagged FGF (Branchless, Bnl) produced in the wing imaginal disc is transported to the air-sac primordium (ASP) via FGFR-containing cyton extending from ASP cells. The number of FGF-receiving cyton decreases with distance from the source, creating a gradient. FGF acts as a morphogen in ASP, inducing gene expression of pointed-P1 (high) and cut (low) in a concentration-dependent manner. Transcription factors Pointed-P1 and Cut antagonistically regulate FGFR cyton formation, generating regions with varying cyton density. This feedback loop self-generates tissue-specific FGF gradients, adapting to ASP morphology. Contrary to diffusion-based models, cyton-mediated transport enables precise, dynamic gradients for morphogenesis. Findings highlight cyton-dependent signaling as a mechanism for long-range, adaptable patterning in development.
This study reveals a novel α/β coiled coil structure formed by inserting two or six residues into the hept repeat of a parallel trimer coiled coil, inducing strain that breaks α-helices into β-strands, creating a β-layer. The α/β coiled retains one backbone hydrogen bond per unit, with a 10° axial shift. β-layers, found in natural coiled, form alternating α-β fibers, a new fold. Minimal α/β uses hexads (3 β,3 in Ramach). First β-layer is 6-residue hex after g, biased to c as T6/T9. α/β is new class with super. Demonstrates coiled flexibility via mutations.
Epsin, an evolutionarily conserved endocytic clathrin adaptor, is critical for clathrin-mediated endocytosis. Conditional triple KO in embryonic fibroblasts revealed cell division defects and impaired endocyt, marked by accumulation of shallow, U-shaped clathrin-coated pits. This correlates with disrupted clathrin-actin coupling, confirmed via cell-free assays. Epsin generates force for pit invagination and fission, alongside SNARE interactions (e.g., synaptobrevin2/VAMP), ensuring vesicle budding and SNARE incorporation. Shallow pit accumulation in KO cells highlights its role in transitioning to deep invaginated pits. Eps couples clathrin to actin via Hip1, aiding invagination. Key functions include clath coat dynamics and SNARE coupling, essential for endocytic progression.
