Zebrafish (Danio rerio) stripe patterns arise from interactions among three pigment cell types: melanophores (dark), xanthores (yellow), and iridoph (reflective). Mutations in leopard (leo), encoding connexin4.8 (Cx4.8), a gap junction subunit, produce spotted phenotypes. A dominant leot3 allele causes complete pattern loss, suggesting a dominant-negative effect on another junction component, identified as connex34 (luchs). Loss-of-function luchs disrupts stripe formation but spares fins. Double mutants and chimeras show leo and luchs act in xanth and melan, not irid, indicating heteromeric junctions of Cx48 and Cx4. Phenotypes suggest homotyp interactions between melan and xanth, instructing irid patterning. leot3 homozygotes or leot3/le1 heterozygotes exhibit dissolved patterns, with melan clusters near irid. Cx48 leot3 mutations (e.g., I52F) impair junctions, implying heterom Cx8/C4 channels mediate chromatoph interactions. Irid expansion in mutants may result from xanth covering. Data confirm Cx8/C4 heterom junctions are critical for pigment patterning.
The study investigates the evolution of insect olfactory receptors in three species: *Lepachis yata* (Archaeognatha), *Thermobia* (Zygent), and *Phyllium* (Phasode). *Lepis* lacks OR and Orco, *Therm* has three Orco candidates, *Phyll* has a full OR/Or system. Indicates ORs evolved later in insects, not as terrestrial adaptation, with Orco predating OR. Previous research focused on neopter insects. ORs are multtransmembrane, distinct from nemate/vertebrates. ORs function as heteromers with Or. Orco emerged post-brist split, OROr post-wings. Concludes ORs arose post-wing evolution, Or pre-OR.
A study in four villages in Kayin State, Myanmar, evaluated the impact of mass antimalarial drug administration (MDA) on *Plasmodium falciparum* transmission. Spatial and epidemiological analysis revealed that neighborhoods with low MDA adherence had 2.8 times higher odds of post-MDA clinical malaria episodes compared to high-adherence areas, independent of individual participation, indicating herd protection. Subclinical infections, high mosquito biting rates, and household exposure to malaria cases also predicted clinical episodes. Despite overall high MDA participation, localized non-adherence clusters hindered elimination. The study documented the first empirical evidence of a herd effect from MDA for *P. falciparum*, with reduced transmission persisting beyond antimal prophylactic duration (1 month). Villages (KNH, TPN, HKT, TOT) were selected via ultrasensitive PCR (uPCR) surveys. Post-MDA increases in clinical cases correlated with higher human biting rates (HBR). Findings support MDA’s role in population-level protection but highlight spatial non-adherence as a barrier to elimination. Results inform policy on optimizing MDA strategies for malaria control.
The study reveals that transcription factor (TF) binding specificities are highly conserved between *Drosophila* and mammals despite 60 million years of evolution, with orthologous TFs sharing core motifs, flanking sequences, and secondary binding modes, including subtle dinucleotide preferences. Analysis of 222 *Drosophila* TFs via HT-LEX and comparison to human/mouse data showed conservation of complex binding repertoires, challenging assumptions of rapid evolutionary drift. Divergent TF specificities in humans correlate with cell types absent in *Drosophila*, suggesting TF evolution contributes to novel cell differentiation. Structural families align with similar binding, yet some TFs exhibit divergent roles (e.g., bZIP, HOX, nuclear receptors). Findings highlight TF specificity as stable yet adaptable, with divergence linked to cellular innovation.
Researchers developed a human-induced pluripotent stem cell (hiPSC) model to study cardio-pulmonary co-development by simultaneously differentiating mesodermal cardiac and endodermal lineages via temporal WNT/nodal signaling in 3D culture. Concentric microissues (μTs) formed, with cardiac aiding alve maturation. W withdrawal caused segregation into distinct cardiac/pul μTs. This model enables investigating embryonic crosstalk and boundary formation.
Escherichia coli dynamically adjusts mutation rates in response to ethanol-induced stress, balancing robustness, and evolvability. Hypermutation arises during high ethanol exposure, enabling rapid adaptation, but declines once stress is mitigated. Mutation rates fluctuate transitorily, increasing under stress and decreasing post-adaptation. Cellular mortality drives this evolution, with higher death rates selecting for transient hypermutator states to accelerate adaptation. Experimental evolution and mutator strains revealed optimal mutation rates for ethanol tolerance. Findings explain hypermutation’s role in antibiotic resistance and cancer relapse, highlighting mortality as a key driver of mutation dynamics. Stress-induced mutation flexibility challenges the view of mutation rates as static, showing rapid evolution under near-lethal conditions.
This study identifies the Arabidopsis transcription factor bZIP3 as a key regulator of the low-energy response in plants, directly phosphorylated by the SnRK1 kinase. SnRK-mediated phosphorylation of three conserved serine residues in bZIP3’s N- and C-terminus alters its dimerization preferences, modulating target gene expression and primary metabolism. bZIP3 knockout mutants exhibit starvation-related phenotypes, including metabolic and senescence defects, which are rescued by wild-type bZIP3 but not a phosphorylation-defective S9/S24/S30A mutant. The SnRK kinase AKIN0 drives starvation-induced bZIP3 hyperphosphorylation, establishing bZIP3 as the first transcription factor directly targeted by SnRK in plant energy deprivation. Phosphorylation at these sites is critical for bZIP3’s dimerization and activity, with a phosphorylation-triggered dimer switch model proposed to regulate metabolic reprogramming. Sugar- and energy-dependent phosphorylation of bZIP3 was confirmed via genomic complementation experiments, showing wild-type but not mutant bZIP rescues delayed gene induction of ASN1, DIN1, and ProDH in b3 mutants. These findings reveal bZIP3 as a central metabolic regulator under stress/starvation, with SnRK-mediated phosphorylation as a conserved mechanism for transcriptional energy response, linking kinase activity to dimerization and gene regulation. The work clarifies SnRK’s dual role in enzyme modulation and transcriptional control, advancing understanding of plant energy homeostasis. (DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.0888.26)
This study identifies RING finger protein 0 (RNF0) as a synapse-to-nucleus messenger mediating transcriptional responses to NMDA receptor (NAR) activation in hippocampal neurons. RNF0 localizes at excitatory synapses, binding the Glu2 subunit of NARs, and translocates to the nucleus via importin-dependent transport upon synaptic Glu2-containing NAR activation and LTP induction. Nuclear R0 is essential for LTP maintenance and structural spine plasticity; its silencing impairs LTP and reduces spine density. R0 dissociates from NAR complexes activity-dependently, utilizing the Glu2 cytoplasmic tail (aa 91–129) shared with CaM. Synaptic, not extrasynaptic, NAR activation drives R0 nuclear accumulation, linking synaptic signals to nuclear gene expression. R0’s role in spine remodeling depends on syn-to-nus signaling, not synaptic levels, highlighting NAR as a hub for nuclear messengers. This pathway encodes synaptic Glu2 NAR signals to the nucleus, potentially docking signalosomes at nuclear targets, underscoring syn-to-nus communication in synaptic plasticity.
The study presents the crystal structure of the N-terminal domain of the Swi/Snf ATPase Mot1 bound to the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex at 3.8 Å resolution, revealing that Mot1 induces conformational changes by reducing DNA:NC interactions and unbending DNA, priming TBP:NC2 displacement without ATP. Electron microscopy and cross-linking show Mot1’s Swi/S2nf domain binds upstream DNA and NC’s histone fold, mirroring nucleosome remodelers. The interface between Mot1 and NC, though small, facilitates separation of NC’s TBP anchor helix from TBP. This provides the first pseudoatomic view of a Swi/Snf ATPase interacting with a DNA:protein complex, elucidating how it destabilizes protein:DNA interfaces. Structural and biochemical data suggest ATP-independent conformational priming precedes ATP-dependent dissociation, offering a framework for Swi/Snf mechanisms in chromatin remodeling.
Researchers designed synthetic variants of ternatin, a cytotoxic natural product, with up to 50-fold higher potency against cancer cells. Using a photo-affinity probe, they identified the eukaryotic elongation factor-1 ternary complex (e1·GTP·aminoacyl-tRNA) as a target. Unrelated natural products (didemnin, ansen) competed for binding. Mutations in e1 domain III blocked ternatin binding and conferred resistance, implicating a hydrophobic surface as critical. Ternatin variants inhibit protein synthesis, correlating with cytotoxicity, and bind e1 only in its ternary complex, a first reported small-molecule interaction. Structural simplicity and accessibility suggest ternatins as drug leads. Four divergent e1 inhibitors highlight its role in cytotoxicity.
This study investigates the role of the inositol 5-phosphatase OCRL in clathrin-mediated endocytosis using fibroblasts from Lowe syndrome patients, a condition caused by OCRL mutations. OCRL, known to interact with clathrin, is typically recruited to late-stage clathrin-coated pits. The absence of OCRL in patient cells impairs endocytic clath coat dynamics, leading to an endocytic defect marked by accumulation of clathrin-coated vesicles and U-shaped pits, likely due to coat sequestration on uncoated vesicles. These vesicles retain PI(4,5)P2 and nucleate numerous actin comets. SNX9, an adaptor linking late pits to actin polymerization and binding OCRL, remains associated with these vesicles, indicating OCRL's role in unco. The findings suggest OCRL facilitates unco and recycling of endoc factors, essential for endoc progression. The defect in unco delays endoc, stalls pits, and triggers actin nucleation from uncoated vesicles. This links OCRL dysfunction to clath endoc defects in Lowe syndrome, previously unreported in fibbl. Time-lapse microscopy confirmed altered clath-co pit dynamics in patients. The presence of actin comets driven by clrin ves in Lowe fibbl supports a clath-actin link, relevant to endoc. The study reveals OCRL's role in unco and shows cl endoc defects contribute to Lowe pathology. DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.0957.0275.
This study identifies a novel regulatory mechanism by which focal adhesion kinase (FAK) and proline-rich tyrosine kinase 2 (PYK2) drive Wnt/β-caten signaling in intestinal tumorigenesis. In APC-mut mice and human colorectal cancer, elevated FAK/PY2 phosphorylate glycogen kinase 3β (GSKβ) at tyrosine 26 (Y26), stabilizing β-caten and promoting tumor growth. Phosphorylation of GSKY26 enhances its interaction with β-TrCP, a ubiquitin ligase required for β-c degradation, thereby increasing β-c accumulation. In APC-mice, loss of APC upregulates FAK/PY2, which redundantly phosphorylate GSKY6 to sustain Wnt signaling. Pharmacological inhibition of FAK/PY2 with PF-627 in APC-mice reduced adenomas, β-c levels, and GY6 phosphorylation, confirming the axis’s role in tumorigenesis. While GY6 phosphorylation was previously noted, this study links it directly to FAK/PY2 activity. Despite redundancy in CRC cells, intestinal FAK deletion alone abrogated adenomas in APC-mut mice, suggesting context-dependent roles. Findings highlight FAK/PY2/GSKY6 as a critical node in APC-driven Wnt activation, offering potential therapeutic targets for colorectal cancer.
The study examines how the vestibular system regulates balance during transitions between standing and locomotion or postures. Electrical vestibular stimulation (EVS) and time-frequency analysis of ground reaction forces in healthy subjects revealed discontinuities in vestibular responses preceding movement onset and termination. Results show humans suspend vestibular stabilizing mechanisms prior to transitions, indicating a shift in control policies as per optimal feedback theory. This occurs in locomotion initiation and posture shifts, suggesting a general process. Suspension relates to differing sensory feedback needs between states. Demonstrates 'stop balancing before moving' and 'stop moving before balancing'. Vestibular suspension occurs before movement onset while upright, reflecting policy disengagement for new control.
The circadian transcription factor Rpa in cyanobacteria is essential for survival in light/dark cycles but not constant light, as its deletion (rA) impairs carbon reserve accumulation during the day and utilization at night, leading to energy deficits and inviability. The rA strain fails to store glycogen and activate catabolism genes at dusk, disrupting energy homeostasis. Providing external carbon or reconstructing pathways restores viability, showing circadian regulation by Rpa temporally coordinates anabol and catabol to prepare for darkness. This anticipatory metabolism ensures energy during resource-limited periods, highlighting the circadian system’s role in fitness under environmental cycles.
The study presents the first structural and functional characterization of a PIB-4 ATPase, CoT from *Sulfitobacter sp. NAS41-1*, revealing an eight-helix transmembrane domain architecture lacking heavy-metal-binding domains (HBDs) and elucidating its ion-transport mechanism. CoT exhibits dual roles in heavy-metal release and histidine as counterion, with potassium-independent turnover distinct from other P-type ATPases. Functional assays confirm broad metal specificity, including Zn²⁺, Ni²⁺, Cu⁺, and Ca², aligning with diverse PIB-4-ATP roles in cellular homeostasis and detox. The absence of HBD challenges prior assumptions, suggesting alternative metal-sensing mechanisms. Inhibitory compounds identified offer potential for drug development targeting PIB-4-ATases, which act as virulence factors in pathogens. This work establishes foundational insights into PIB-4-ATP structure and function, critical for manipulating metal homeostasis in biotechnological and therapeutic contexts.
A study identifies a chromatin signature associated with mitotically stable monoallelic expression (MAE) in mammalian genes, characterized by co-occurrence of H3K363me (active) and H3273me (repressive) marks in gene bodies. This signature is present in ∼2% of ubiquitously expressed genes and 3–8% of tissue-specific genes across cell types, enriched in developmental genes with bivalent chromatin in pluripotent cells. The approach, independent of SNPs and clonality, enables MAE analysis in polycl samples, revealing MAE in 20% of ubiquitously expressed and 3–4% tissue-specific genes. Bivalent genes, silent in pluripotent cells, acquire MAE chromatin in differentiated cells, suggesting MAE links to differentiation. MAE chromatin predicts allelic bias in monoclonal lines, detectable in polycl populations. Findings indicate MAE is widespread, tied to gene regulation during differentiation, and detectable via chromatin marks. DOI: http://dx.doi.org/10.754/eLife.026.04.
This study reveals a regulatory mechanism by which coiled-coil domains control AAA+ chaperone Clp activity in bacteria. Cryo-EM structures show Clp forms inactive helical assemblies via head-to-head coiled-coil interactions in its resting state, restricting substrate binding and ATP hydrolysis. Adaptor Mec binds to coiled-coil domains, converting Clp to an active planar ring, enabling substrate engagement and ATPase activation. Mutations in coiled domains cause constitutive activation, leading to cellular toxicity, highlighting their role in repression. This mechanism, observed in Clp, ClE, and ClL, suggests a conserved regulatory strategy for bacterial AAA+ chaperones. Disrupting coiled repression could target virulence and stress pathways, offering antimicrobial potential. Coiled domains, previously known for head-tail repression in Clp/Hsp0, here act head-to-head, expanding their regulatory roles in ATPase control.
This study investigates how dormant adult muscle precursors (AMPs) in *Drosophila* are reactivated using a muscle-derived signaling cascade. AMPs maintain filopodia contact with neighboring muscles during quiescence and proliferation, suggesting a niche role for muscles in reactivation. Genetic analyses reveal that muscles secrete inductive dIl6 signals, activating the Insulin pathway in adjacent AMPs, which triggers Notch and its target dMyc to drive proliferation. The Insulin-Not-dyc cascade governs AMP reactivation, with dM directly regulating proliferation. AMPs, transient stem cells in *Drosophila*, remain dormant until larval stages, then activate to generate myoblasts for muscle growth/regeneration. dM’s role mirrors vertebrate Myc in proliferation, indicating conserved mechanisms. Experiments confirm Notch functions downstream of Insulin and dyc downstream of Notch. Muscles act as a niche, initiating the Insulin/Not-dyc cascade to reactivate AMPs in later larval stages. Findings highlight niche-driven signaling as critical for AMP state transitions, linking developmental and regenerative processes in *Drosophila* and vertebrates. (206 words)
The study identifies the sodium leak channel NALCN as critical for maintaining the spontaneous firing of GABAergic neurons in the substantia nigra reticulata (SNr), a key component of the basal ganglia output. Using single-cell RNA sequencing, researchers found NALCN to be the most highly expressed non-selective cation channel in SNr neurons, suggesting its role in generating the depolarizing leak current necessary for tonic firing. Experiments showed SNr neurons lacking NAL exhibited impaired spontaneous activity, indicating NAL's essential role in sustaining their baseline excitability. NAL also modulates neuronal excitability in response to glycolysis changes and muscarinic acetylcholine receptor activation. These findings suggest NAL disruption could impair basal ganglia function, potentially causing motor deficits in humans with NAL mutations. The study highlights NAL's importance in maintaining SNr activity, which is crucial for proper inhibition of downstream brain areas. NAL's interaction with proteins UNC8, UNC9, and FAM15A further supports its functional significance. Disruption of SNr firing, as seen in NAL deficiency, may lead to dystonia or Parkinsonian-like hyperactivity, emphasizing NAL's role in motor control.
This study introduces a droplet-based microfluidic system for high-throughput cultivation and screening of antibiotic-producing microbes, addressing challenges in culturing recalcitrant bacteria. By miniaturizing cultivation to 20.2 pL droplets, enabling 2 million single-cell compartments in 1.5 mL, the method achieves higher bacterial diversity and uncultured species recovery than agar. Coupled with in-droplet antimicrobial screening using reporter strains, it isolates active producers and identifies secreted metabolites, maintaining genotype-phenotype linkage. Environmental conditions are mimicked, and droplets stored for controlled incubation. Despite axenic culture limitations post-isolation, genomic data is obtained. The approach streamlines antibiotic discovery by linking cultivation, activity screening, and biosynthesis potential, offering scalable access to untapped microbial chemical diversity.
Tuberculous meningitis (TBM) has high mortality linked to excessive inflammation, yet adjunctive corticosteroids reduce mortality by only 3%, suggesting inflammation drives a subset of deaths. In Vietnam, anti-inflammatory corticosteroids improved survival most in patients with a C/T variant in the leukotriene A4 hydrolase (LTAH) gene, which regulates inflammatory eicosanoids. LTAH TT homozygotes (TT) showed increased survival, aligning with corticosteroids benefiting hyper-inflammatory responses. However, an Indonesian study found no LTAH TT benefit. Bayesian analysis of both cohorts revealed LTA TT conferred early and sustained survival benefits in both populations, but this effect was nullified in severe cases with high early mortality. LTA genotyping combined with disease severity could target glucocortic therapy to those likely to benefit. The discrepancy in Indonesia may stem from severe disease overriding LTA effects or dysregulated inflammation unmeasured by standard metrics. Severe disease may negate corticosteroid benefits, as seen in Indonesian cohort’s higher mortality. Findings suggest LTA genotype influences inflammation and corticosteroid response, supporting genotype-directed therapy. Severe BM may resist interventions, highlighting need for severity-genotype integration in treatment.
The study identifies Shootin1 as a mechano-effector of Netrin-1 signaling in axon guidance. Shootin1 knockout mice exhibit forebrain commissural axon misprojection, mirroring Netrin1 deficiency. Netrin gradients induce polarized Shootin phosphorylation in growth cones, enhancing its interaction with L1-CAM and promoting F-actin–adhesion coupling to generate migratory forces. Spatially regulated phosphorylated Shootin is essential for axon turning but not outgrowth in Netrin gradients. Disrupting this phosphorylation or F-act-adhesion coupling inhibits Netrin-induced axon turning. Shootin–L-CAM interactions mediate Netrin guidance beyond L1-CAM substrates. Phosphorylation dynamics link Netrin gradients to directional mechanoresponse.
Researchers used acoustic holography and aerodynamic measurements to show hummingbirds’ hum timbre arises from oscillating lift/drag forces. Model links aerodynamics and kinematics, revealing harmonics supporting bodyweight generate timbre. Allometric scaling: larger birds quieter, elongated insects louder. Oscillating forces drive acoustic signatures in flapping.
The study investigates the roles of chance, contingency, and necessity in protein evolution using ancestral reconstruction and experimental evolution of BCL-2 family proteins to replicate historical protein–protein interaction (PPI) specificity changes. By evolving multiple ancestral proteins under identical selection pressures, researchers found that contingency—arising from historical substitutions altering available mutational pathways—dominated over necessity as the driver of sequence variation over long timescales. Trajectories from phylogenetically distant ancestors produced no shared mutations, even under strong selection, highlighting contingency’s erasure of necessity. Chance emerged because multiple mutational sets could alter specificity at any time, while historical contingency reshaped these options. Results indicate BCL-2 sequences (and likely others) are idiosyncratic products of unpredictable history, shaped by chance and contingency. Present-day proteins reflect accumulated historical events constrained by physics, chemistry, and selection. Additional biological factors may limit functional genotypes, reducing chance’s role, but experiments suggest adaptive paths to PPIs changed repeatedly during BCL-2 evolution, even when binding profiles were stable. Findings underscore contingency as a primary force in evolutionary outcomes, alongside selection and drift, revealing evolutionary trajectories as contingent on historical accidents rather than deterministic necessity.
This study uses genome-wide haplotype-based analysis to characterize genetic diversity and gene flow in eight sub-Saharan African populations. Coastal groups exhibit Eurasian haplotype influx over the past 70 years, while Eastern and Southern Niger-Congo speakers share ancestry with Central Africans due to recent expansions. Most sub-Saharan populations have ancestry from outside their region, reflecting gene flow in the last40 years. Haplotype sharing across ethno-linguistic groups and allele movement into new environments are relevant to epidemiology. Eurasian admixture occurred across timescales, supported by AGVP data and Tishoff et al. Differentiation aligns with self-reported ancestry. Admixture common in human populations. Relevance to genotype-phenotype associations.
The study elucidates how mitotic spindles scale with cell size during Xenopus development. Using in vitro cytoplasmic extracts from stages 3 and 8, researchers found kinesin-3 KifA’s microtubule-destabilizing activity is inhibited in stage3 by cytoplasmic importin α, which binds Kif, but activated in stage8 when importin partitions to membrane, freeing Kif to reduce spindle size. Altered spindles impaired orientation but not segregation. Spindle size coupled to cell via rati of micro-destabil. Importin, regulated by phosphorylation, controls Kif. Kif ΔLS shows higher importin in stage3. Mechanism links size via partitioning, relevant vertebrates. Consistent meiotic-mit transitions. Spindle via importin-Kif.
The article reports the discovery of tandem riboswitches containing guanine and phosphoribosyl pyrophosphate (PRPP) aptamers that function as an IMPLY Boolean logic gate to regulate purine biosynthesis in bacteria. These RNA elements bind guanine and PR independently or together, modulating transcription termination via structural changes in their expression platform, which stabilizes or destabilizes terminator/antiterminator stems based on ligand concentrations. This is the first example of tandem apters with distinct ligand specificities on a shared platform, enabling novel logic control. The system reflects RNA’s capacity for complex metabolic regulation without proteins, supporting the RNA World hypothesis, where RNA likely managed metabolism pre-protein. PRPP, a precursor for RNA building blocks, may have been sensed by ancient apters, some persisting as modern riboswitches. Previously overlooked PRPP ribos due to similarity to guanidine ribos are now validated. Tandem systems highlight RNA’s potential for molecular complexity, with in vitro data showing population-level logic outputs. The study underscores RNA’s role in ancient metabolite sensing and suggests undiscovered logic gates await in modern cells.
The study demonstrates that the number of neurotransmitter vesicles released per action potential is regulated by the size of the readily-releasable pool (RRP), not solely by vesicle release probability. Using mouse brain slices, researchers found that protein kinase A (PKA) and its target synapsin dynamically control RRP occupancy, determining vesicle count without altering release probability. This challenges the prevailing view that calcium-dependent release probability dictates unives (UVR) or multivesicular (MVR) release. High-probability synapses can exhibit UVR, while low-probability synapses may show MVR, indicating RRP as the critical factor. The RRP reflects release-competent vesicles per active zone, constrained by synapsin. This mechanism explains synaptic diversity in release modes (UVR/MVR) and activity-dependent switching. Findings redefine presynaptic regulation, highlighting RRP as a key determinant of synaptic signaling variability.
HIV-1 Gag selectively packages dimeric, unspliced viral RNA from a cytosolic RNA pool by assembling on membranes. Using giant unilellar vesicles (GUVs), recombinant myristylated Gag, and purified RNAs, researchers reconstit selective packaging of the HIV-1 5’UTR in excess competitor RNA. Membrane-bound Gag multimerization via lattice contacts is critical for RNA selectivity; mutations in the CA-CT domain disrupting self-assembly abolish selectivity. Gag bound to viral RNA suppresses tRNA binding, while tRNA inhibits genome-less assembly. The NC domain is necessary but insufficient for selectivity. Membrane-localized Gag assembly drives genomic RNA recruitment without altering its structure, with monomeric RNAs coalescing to dimers on membranes in Gag-expressing cells. This interdependence of Gag self-assembly and RNA selectivity suggests a model where viral RNA with high-affinity Gag sites recruits multimeric Gag to membranes, excluding tRNA. The study clarifies how cytos Gag, primarily bound to cellular RNA, assembles into particles packaging dimeric viral RNA with 9% efficiency, underscoring membrane events in HIV-1 genome selection.
